==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
With the introduction of new many-core architectures in computers, there is
increased interest in fast and scalable algorithms that can take advantage of the
massive concurrency available on these new architectures. However, program-
ming for these architectures present the challenge of developing methods that
can take advantage of that concurrency, and then implementing those methods
so they run fast using multi-threaded programming.
Additionally, there has always been an interest in solving Partial Di’¼Ćerential
Equations (PDEs) e’¼āciently and with su’¼ācient accuracy.
They show up in
many applications in engineering. Numerical solution techniques are often cho-
sen, which can solve discretizations of PDEs. However, solutions with many
unknowns take a longer time to compute.
This report deals with the The Full Approximation Scheme (FAS), which is a
non-linear multigrid technique that uses a sequence of grids of di’¼Ćerent reso-
lution to achieve O(n) computational complexity for solving elliptic boundary
value PDEs. In this work, focus is on a non-linear elliptic boundary value PDE
on the unit square domain using uniform discretization and a 5-point star sten-
cil, which the method will be tied to. Here, n is the number of cells on the
discretized grid covering the domain.
The goal of this thesis is to create a proof of concept by combining the e’¼āciency
of FAS with the powerful modern many-core architectures to be able to solve this
type of PDE very quickly. This, in turn, allows high resolution in the solution.
In thi work, we consider using multilevel FAS with the Jacobi smoother, Gauss-
==PAGE:4==
ii
Seidel smoother, Chebyshev Polynomial smoother, and the Conjugate Gradient
smoother. NewtonŌĆÖs Method was also introduced for comparison. Implemen-
tations of these methods that can take advantage of the massive concurrency
possible today were made using C and OpenMP for speed and e’¼āciency.
Two stages of benchmarking were carried out. The ’¼ürst applied sequential ver-
sions of the solution methods proposed to non-linear elliptic boundary value
PDEs on discretizations with up to 8192 ├Ś 8192 cells, varying how much the
non-linear part dominated the linear part of the PDE.
This established NewtonŌĆÖs Method with sparse LU-factorization as a quadrat-
ically convergent method with a computational complexity of at best O(n1.5),
and multilevel FAS as a linearly convergent method with a computational com-
plexity of O(n). It was also determined that FAS can be implemented such
that it uses very little memory compared to NewtonŌĆÖs Method with sparse LU-
factorization.
The second benchmark was carried out on a modern Haswell processor from
Intel with 4 physical cores using up to 8 threads, as well as IntelŌĆÖs MIC archi-
tecture, otherwise known as the Xeon Phi, with 60 physical cores and very high
memory bandwidth using up to 244 threads. Here, as many features as could
be identi’¼üed of these architectures were used, including of course the massive
levels of concurrency possible due to the amount of physical cores available as
well SIMD and vectorization. The same problems were tested as in the ’¼ürst
phase.
The study showed good weak and strong scaling when using Chebyshev Polyno-
mial or Gauss-Seidel smoothing for the Haswell processor from 1 physical core to
all available 4 physical cores, with the speed-up reaching nearly 400% compared
to 1 core when including Hyperthreading.
It showed that Chebyshev Polynomial and Gauss-Seidel smoothing scaled ap-
proximately equally well with the number of cores, but Chebyshev Polynomial
smoothing took twice as long for every test. However, Chebyshev Polynomial
smoothing does not require vertex colouring. It also showed that the Conjugate
Gradient method was very fast on one core, but did not show as high strong or
weak scaling for the method when used across multiple cores.
On a Xeon Phi co-processor from Intel, the study showed good strong scaling
up to 32 physical cores for all methods, particularly for Gauss-Seidel smoothing.
Beyond that, the study highlighted di’¼āculties achieving great speed-ups using
all the physical cores, and showed a decrease in speed-up when using multiple
threads per core. More studies are warranted for investigating more closely why
the algorithm had these di’¼āculties, and how they may be corrected.

Preface
This thesis was prepared in collaboration with DTU Compute in ful’¼ülment of
the requirements for acquiring an M.Sc. in Engineering. It deals with the Full
Approximation Scheme (FAS), which is a multigrid technique useful for solving
non-linear elliptic boundary value problems.
I would like to extend my gratitude to the advisers on the project, namely
Associate Professor Allan P. Engsig-Karup from DTU Compute, master of sci-
ence Max la cour Christensen, and master of science Stefan Lemvig Glimberg
- not only for their immense assistance in the forming of the project, but also
in helping me solve some of the problems I ran into along the way. Without
their assistance, the crafting of this master of science thesis would not have been
possible.
Secondly, I would like to send LloydŌĆÖs Registry Consulting my gratitude, as they
have helped me a great deal by trusting me and putting me in an environment
of likeminded people working on similar projects who were able to supply aid
in understanding a concept whenever I asked.
I would also like to thank DTU Compute for their willingness to let me borrow a
variety of computing devices to test my software on and for meeting my request
to write my thesis with them.
And last, but certainly not least, I would like to thank my family for their
incredible moral support and patience with me during this time.
At times,
when stress was starting to catch up and loneliness was starting to settle in,
they were there to keep me comfortable and happy.

All of these people really rose the occasion and showed unmatched patience and
discipline. Thank you all very much.

Chapter 1
Motivation and
Introduction
Partial Di’¼Ćerential Equations (PDE) that can only be solved numerically show
up all the time in engineering - whether it is in the ’¼üelds of mathematics, physics,
chemistry, or any number of other areas. According to Henson et al [Hen15]
Multigrid Methods have become a common approach to solving many of these
types of problems.
Multigrid methods are a class of solution techniques that can be applied to PDEs
on continuous spatial domains. When applied to such a problem, the methods
use a hierarchy is grids to reduce the computational complexity to O(n), where
n is the number of vertices on the grid. This is much faster than a more di-
rect method, but it needs to be optimized for the speci’¼üc problem it is solving.
Notably it is a powerful solution technique for elliptic boundary problems, ac-
cording to [TOS00]. Brandt [Bra84] puts the motivation for Multigrid in terms
of the "golden rule":
The amount of computational work should be proportional to the
amount of real physical changes in the computed system. Stalling
numerical processes must be wrong.
Common examples of the kind of stalling Brandt [Bra84] talks about are the
==PAGE:14==
2
Motivation and Introduction
algebraic equations arising from a discretization of partial di’¼Ćerential equations
- particularly those of the non-linear variety, in which many direct solution
iterations are required to solve the problem to any real accuracy.
This can become a particularly large problem if one desires to solve a particularly
sti’¼Ćproblem, i.e. there may be several solution components with di’¼Ćerent scales,
which con’¼éict with one another. The smooth or small components may be easily
solved with a coarse grid discretization of the partial di’¼Ćerential equation, but
the non-smooth or large components cannot, resulting in a situation where lots
of computational power may be needed to attain accuracy, but is in some ways
wasted, solving the smooth part of the problem, gaining only small corrections
on a massively large grid.
In this report, focus will be on solving the non-linear elliptic model problem is
de’¼üned as:

Furthermore, by

deal with sti’¼Ćness and varying degrees of non-linearity. The methods presented
should also be applicable to other domains with minor modi’¼ücation.
Max la cour Christensen [lCCE12], one of my supervisors introduced the prob-
lem of e’¼ācient Oil Reservoir Simulation in the early stages of this project.
Finding and extracting oil in an e’¼ācient manner is an enormous challenge, and
the challenge will only grow as more and more oil is extracted, making the re-
maining oil more scarce and di’¼ācult to ’¼ünd. However, [lCCE12] ’¼ünds that the
FAS technique can be applied to the problem to solve it very e’¼āciently, but does
not manage to take advantage of the massive concurrency of modern many-core
processors.
Oil Reservoir Simulation will not be treated directly treated in this report, but
it is a motivation to be creating an implementation of the Full Approximation
Scheme that can take advantage of modern many-core architectures.
==PAGE:15==
1.1 Modern Many-core Architectures
3
1.1
Modern Many-core Architectures
From some of the earliest days of computing, the targets and expectations of the
computer industry were dominated by MooreŌĆÖs Law, described in the famous
paper by IntelŌĆÖs co-founder Gordon E. Moore [Moo65]. MooreŌĆÖs Law was an
observation that the number of transistors in a dense integrated circuit would
double approximately every two years.
While MooreŌĆÖs Law was merely a guess on how the industry would develop,
chip makers across the globe decided to target the expectation set by MooreŌĆÖs
Law, making the prediction a self-ful’¼ülling prophecy according to the New York
Times, [Fly04].
The "law" was often cited as meaning that the processing power of the Central
Processing Units (CPU) designed by Intel, and many other chip manufacturers,
would double approximately every 18 months.
According to [Fly04], Intel intended to increase performance primarily by in-
creasing the clock frequency of their processors, leading to very high sequential
performance. The article mentions that, in 2004, Intel were slated to come up
with the Jayhawk architecture, which was expected to have incredibly high clock
frequency, but also to be entirely single threaded. However, in May 2004, Intel
gave up on this idea and started focusing on parallel computing, because it was
vastly easier to improve performance using parallelism than it was using entirely
serial processing because of heat issues. Figure 1.1, as seen on [Hru12], shows
precisely what happened about 10 years ago, and this trend has continued well
into 2015.
From this moment onward, parallel computing became increasingly important
to achieve high levels of performance. Today, this often comes in the form of
processors with many "cores" that all share the same system memory, but also
each have their own small caches of private memory. This is commonly referred
to as a "Shared Memory Multicore Processor".
This process of making CPUŌĆÖs more and more parallel while keeping clock fre-
quencies down quite recently culminated in the development of the Xeon Phi.
This processor is described as an Intel co-processor that focuses on parallel com-
puting by presenting 60 cores that are each relatively slow, but are very powerful
when combined with high memory bandwidth and a well written program that
takes advantage of this massive concurrency, as noted by Jim Je’¼Ćers et al [JJ13].
When developing for such an architecture, there are several major challenges
according to [Rot05]:
==PAGE:16==
4
Motivation and Introduction
Figure 1.1: Intel CPU power trends from 1970 to 2010.
1. Su’¼ācient computation order independence - Since there are multiple threads,
it must be possible to carry out multiple computations at the same time
independently of one another.
2. Thread synchronization - Local data dependencies must be protected by
Thread synchronization. Threads must communicate, telling each other
when they have ’¼ünished computing the current batch of order-independent
operations before the next batch of operations can start execution.
3. Parallel Data Access - Every thread needs a local copy of the data it can
read from and write to. All the threads may read from any part of memory,
but the threads may not write to any memory another thread needs to
read from concurrently.
[Rot05] describes that when any of these conditions fail to be met, a symptom
known as a "data race" often comes up. Typically it manifests itself as the
wrong result being written into memory, because two or more cores disagreed
on what was to be written at that memory location.
These factors make implementing a parallel version of FAS more di’¼ācult, but
due to the factors given previously, it could also be very rewarding.
==PAGE:17==
1.2 Objectives
5
However, a survey of the current published literature has revealed that the
method FAS does not appear to be heading into the world of parallel computing
in the way that linear Multigrid is. In order to apply Multigrid to non-linear
problems e’¼āciently, this topic should be explored in depth.
Implementing the Full Approximation Scheme necessitates developing the vari-
ous components required for it to function. Primarily, new smoothing techniques
will have to be considered. While traditional techniques, such as Gauss-Seidel,
can be parallelized, they present several irritating challenges, and some down-
right limits, when used on non-uniform grids.
According to [WWP09], an algorithm with O(n) complexity is extremely memory-
bound. That is, it will only run fast if given high speed access to memory. To
that end, this report introduces non-linear and e’¼ācient smoothing techniques
and attempts to deploy these techniques on an Intel Xeon Phi, which is a co-
processor from Intel which presents 60 physical cores and extremely high mem-
ory bandwidth, according to IntelŌĆÖs Ark website [Cor15].
1.2
Objectives
The objective of this project is to design and implement a fast Full Approxima-
tion Scheme (FAS) implementation for optimal execution on modern many-core
architectures, as well as to investigate that implementation via testing it on an
ellipctic non-linear boundary value problem.
To do this, several objectives will need to be ful’¼ülled:
ŌĆó Introduce an elliptic non-linear boundary value problem on the square unit
domain and discretize it on a grid. The problem should be constructed
such that the resulting discretization must give rise to a non-linear set of
equations.
ŌĆó Introduce, implement, and test NewtonŌĆÖs Method and use it to solve the
discrete PDE.
ŌĆó Introduce, implement, and test the Full Approximation Scheme and use
it to solve the same discrete PDE.
ŌĆó Compare the performance of these two methods algorithmically and com-
putationally to determine whether FAS is a substantial improvement over
a very simple method.
==PAGE:18==
6
Motivation and Introduction
ŌĆó Use modern many-core architectures in the implementations to make solv-
ing the problem fast.
ŌĆó Attempt to improve the Full Approximation Scheme with new smoothing
methods to make parallel computation even faster.
ŌĆó Test performance and scaling in various ways for this parallel implemen-
tation of FAS to ’¼ünd out how viable it is to implement it on a modern
many-core architecture.
1.2.1
Novel Contributions
The ’¼ürst novel contribution of this work is the application and study of Cheby-
shev Polynomial smoothing for non-linear relaxation in a FAS context. Due to
the smoothing method using no inner products, it should scale very well with
the number of processing units in a parallel setting, which is an attractive qual-
ity in this time where various problems have conspired to make it di’¼ācult to
achieve high sequential performance in computer processors.
The second novel contribution is using the Full Approximation Scheme on a
modern many-core architecture by using multi-threaded programming, and in
general optimizing the code using the latest compiler technology. Despite there
being plenty of work out there showing Linear Multigrid techniques being opti-
mized for modern many-core architectures using multi-threaded programming,
the same cannot be said for the Full Approximation Scheme.
The ’¼ünal novel contribution is an investigation of the performance of a multi-
threaded implementation of FAS on IntelŌĆÖs Many Integrated Core architecture,
such as the Xeon Phi.
==PAGE:19==
1.2 Objectives
7
1.2.2
Project Plan and Self-Assessment
As a starting point, the objective was to implement and understand the Full
Approximation Scheme ’¼ürst.
Originally, there were discussions of using an existing reservoir simulation code
using PETSc to carry out very advanced studies towards the end of the project.
However, over time, the project changed into being a high performance and
parallel implementation of the Full Approximation Scheme for shared memory
systems instead.
Figure 1.2 shows how the project plan ended up after all was said and done.
This is an accurate portrayal of how much time was spent on each part of the
report and accompanying result. The original plan is also given as ’¼üg. 1.3.
The goal of understanding and implementing the Full Approximation Scheme
was ful’¼ülled, and there was also great success in making the implementation run
fast on modern many-core architectures, as well as developing it further with a
couple of novel ideas.

1.3.1
Discretization
Since the above problem is to be used for developing numerical methods to solve
PDEs where the real solution is not known, the problem needs to be discretized.
The choice of discretization method should be chosen for each problem. In this
case, since the problem is simply de’¼üned on a 2D cartesian square domain with
no time component, it seems natural to use a ’¼ünite di’¼Ćerence approximation.
De’¼üne a uniform grid along both the x-direction and the y-direction with grid
spacing h along each direction. Because the grid is de’¼üned on Ōä”= [0, 1] ├Ś [0, 1]
that means there will be N = h+1
h

xi, i = 0, 1, ..., N and yj, j = 0, 1, ..., N.
All unique combinations of these two sets of 1D points represents a point in the
2D plane (xi, yj) = (ih, jh) which will result in a total of (N + 1)2 = (h+1)2
h2
total points. Each of these points has an unknown function value of u(x, y)
associated with it. Each of them is called u(xi, yj) = u(ih, jh) = ui,j.
For each of these points, apply a central second-order ’¼ünite di’¼Ćerence operator
to approximate the Laplacian operator in each of the two cartesian directions.
This becomes a 5-point star stencil on the grid. This stencil is shown on ’¼üg. 1.5.

Motivation and Introduction
For the boundary condition, the model problem states that u(x, y) should be 0
along the boundary, so one can just set u0,j = uN,j = ui,0 = ui,N = 0. This is
a Dirichlet condition.
It is important to note that for ╬│ > 0, the problem becomes non-linear because
of the exponential term multiplied onto ╬│. Because this exponential term is to
the power of the solution ui,j, a large value of ╬│ will lead to a situation where
a a relatively small change in the approximation to the solution will lead to a
very large change in the function value of the left hand side of eq. (1.9).
As Douglas et al [BF93] notes, this forces most numerical solution techniques to
make very small corrections to the approximations at each update (the precise
de’¼ünition of a correction and update are given in section 2.1), or they may
risk the residual, which is the di’¼Ćerence between the result of the left side of the
equation and that on the right, changing so rapidly that the correction massively
overshoots its target, potentially making the residual larger than it already was.
Equations with this property are called sti’¼Ćequations. In other words, the
sti’¼Ćness of the elliptic model problem can be changed by changing ╬│.
From eq. (1.7) the right hand side can be calculated. This source term must
always be known, at least numerically, even if u(x, y) is not.
For each equation in the grid, evaluate fi,j. That would be:
f(xi, yj) = fi,j = ŌłÆ2x2

Chapter 2
NewtonŌĆÖs Method
This section deals with one of many ways to solve the model problem eq. (1.4).
Speci’¼ücally, it deals with NewtonŌĆÖs Method for solving a non-linear partial dif-
ferential equation on a square domain with Dirichlet conditions.
2.1
Introducing NewtonŌĆÖs Method
One method that immediately springs to mind for solving a problem such as
eq. (1.9) is NewtonŌĆÖs Method. As mentioned by Henson et al [Hen15] it is old,
well studied, and widely used. NewtonŌĆÖs Method is an iterative method that
makes repeated uses of Newton steps, also known as Newton iterations, until the
result is su’¼āciently accurate.
Let k denote the current iteration number, i.e. how many Newton steps has
been taken already, starting at 0 where no Newton steps have been taken yet.
Suppose that F : RN ŌåÆRN, N ŌłłN. Now, the objective is to solve the equation
F(x) = 0. By expanding F(x) around an initial guess or approximation of the
==PAGE:26==
14
NewtonŌĆÖs Method
solution xk using a Taylor polynomial, one can get:
F(xk + hk) = F(xk) + hkF ŌĆ▓(xk) + (hk)T hk
2
F ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(╬Š)
(2.1)
= F(xk) + hkJ(xk) + (hk)T hk
2
F ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(╬Š)
(2.2)
where ╬Š is a vector in which each element is a number between the corresponding
element in xk and xk+hk, and J is a function that will give the Jacobian matrix
for F at any point x ŌłłRN.
Neglecting the 2nd order term and assuming F(xk + hk) = 0, then solving for
hk gives:
0 = F(xk) + hkJ(xk) ŌćÆ
hk = ŌłÆ(J(xk))ŌłÆ1F(xk) ŌćÆ
hk = ŌłÆ(Jk)ŌłÆ1rk
where rk is called the residual at the current iteration. It is the result of the
evaluation of F on the current iterate.
Assuming that the neglected 2nd order term is 0, NewtonŌĆÖs Method will solve
the problem as exactly as machine precision allows in a single step.
Otherwise, it has a new iterate, which will be obtained by correcting the current
iterate: xk+1 = xk+hk. This is also known as updating x. After this new iterate
is obtained, NewtonŌĆÖs Method does it all again, increasing k by one until the
answer is good enough. When that exactly is can depend on the implementation
and how the method is to be used. A few ways to determine that the answer is
good enough could be:
1. If ŌłźrkŌłźis below a certain small number, stop and report success.
2. If ŌłźrkŌłź
Ōłźr0Ōłźis below a certain small number, stop and report success.
3. If ŌłźrkŌłź> ŌłźrkŌłÆ1Ōłź, a failure to decrease the residual has occurred - in other
words the solution strategy is moving towards a worse solution than the
one already obtained. This does not necessarily indicate a problem and
can be ’¼üxed via methods such as backtracking or linesearch, but for the
purposes of this implementation, this is considered a problem. Stop and
report failure.
4. Start at iterate k = 0 and evaluate the function. Each time rk and Jk(xk)
are updated, increase k by 1.
If k Ōēźkmax, where kmax ŌłłN, this is
considered stalling. Stop and report failure.

2.2 Solving the Model Problem with NewtonŌĆÖs Method
15
5. If F ŌĆ▓(xk) = 0 or Jk is a singular matrix, this is considered a stationary
point. In this case, the method will attempt to divide by 0. Stop and
report failure.
NewtonŌĆÖs Method can be written algorithmically, as shown in algorithm 1. Note
that it does not keep track of which iteration each residual, estimate of the true
solution, or step belongs to. Other than for debugging reasons, it simply isnŌĆÖt
needed; precisely when the value is updated, the old one is no longer needed.

On the interior points, recall eq. (2.3), now written in terms of the current
approximation vk. On the right hand side, simply write that in as is, in the
order presented on eq. (2.4). For the boundary conditions ri,j = uk

Note that, for this problem, rk and Jk both must be computed anew at every
iteration. This applies generally to any non-linear problem. Parts of the matrix
that are not updated can be saved for later use, so that the entire matrix does
not have to be recalculated every single time. This problem is so simple that
this is not necessary, however. The above results in the sparsity pattern shown
on ’¼üg. 2.1. It should be noted that the resulting matrix is symmetric and also
positive de’¼ünite.

2.4 Computational Complexity
19
2.4
Computational Complexity
The computational complexity of NewtonŌĆÖs Method depends primarily on the
solution method chosen to ’¼ünd the step.
For the implementation presented in this report, LU-decomposition for symmet-
ric matrices was used. The implementation chosen, which is part of LAPACK
and included in OpenBLAS [QXYY13], is based on an iterative version of Sivan
ToledoŌĆÖs recursive LU algorithm, which completes it in O(n3) for a square ma-
trix, where n is width (or height) of the matrix - or in this case the number of
grid points.
This computation will most likely dominate the computational time for New-
tonŌĆÖs Method, as the update operation itself is an O(n) operation (one number
is added to one number for every grid point). The same goes for ’¼ünding the
residual of the norm.
The total runtime of NewtonŌĆÖs Method will be proportional to how many itera-
tions are used. In total, the expected computational complexity is approximately
O(n3)
(2.9)

20
NewtonŌĆÖs Method
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3
Full Approximation Scheme
In this chapter, the Full Approximation Scheme (FAS) will be discussed. Accord-
ing to Brandt [Bra84], FAS belongs to a class of geometric Multigrid methods.
It can be viewed as a generalization of Multigrid methods for the solution of
large linear systems of equations into the solution of large non-linear systems of
equations.
It applies the "two-grid algorithm", possibly recursively, to reduce the resid-
ual.
In doing so, it attempts to reduce the error of an approximation v to
u over one or more iterations. The e’¼āciency of such a method replies on its
components, which include smoothing operations, grid transfer operations that
transfer the residual and correction quantities between a sequence of grids that
are progressively coarser.
3.1
A Multigrid Linear Solver
To introduce FAS, it may be useful to look at a simple linear multigrid method
’¼ürst, intended to solve a problem that, after discretization, results in a linear
system of equations Ju = f. Let v be an approximation to the exact solution
==PAGE:34==
22
Full Approximation Scheme
u, and let the error e = u ŌłÆv and the residual r = f ŌłÆJv. Note that:
r = f ŌłÆJv ŌćÆ
r = f ŌłÆJ (u ŌłÆe) ŌćÆ
r = f ŌłÆJu + Je ŌćÆ
r = Je
(3.1)
Equation (3.1) is known as the residual equation, and according to Henson et
al [Hen15] it is central to the idea presented here.
Before introducing the multigrid idea itself, it is very useful to understand the
two-grid idea.
According to [Hen15], the the two-grid idea is to de’¼üne the
problem on two grids that both approximate the domain Ōä”. One is a ’¼üne grid.
Every grid point in this grid gives rise to a linear equation. The total system
of equations that arises from the discretization of the problem on this ’¼üne grid
can be written as Jhuh = f h. For simplicity in this report, the assumption is
made that the ’¼üne grid is uniform in all cartesian directions and has a distance
h between each point, hence the naming of the variables.
Because computing this result directly may be expensive depending on just
how ’¼üne the grid is, a coarse grid is also de’¼üned. This grid is also uniform in
all cartesian directions, but has the distance H > h between each point. The
corresponding discretization gives rise to the linear set of equations JHuH = f H.
One might refer to JHuH = f H as "the coarse grid problem" and Jhuh = f h as
"the ’¼üne grid problem".
Given these two grids, the "two-grid process" is used:
1. A non-linear relaxation method, such as Gauss-Seidel or Jacobi, is applied
to a ’¼üne-grid problem. This method must have the property that after
relaxation the error will be smooth. Multiple applications, each called a
sweep, may be needed to achieve this. More explanation on why this is
needed can be found in section 3.2.
2. Because the error is smooth, it can su’¼āciently accurately be represented on
a coarser grid. Compute the ’¼üne-grid residual rh and restrict the residual
to the coarse grid using a restriction operator IH
h . That is, rH = IH
h rh.
3. Because the coarse grid has fewer grid points, and therefore fewer un-
knowns, the coarse grid problem is less expensive to solve than the ’¼üne
grid problem. Note now the coarse-grid residual equation JHeH = rH.
This residual equation is solved, which determines the error for the coarse-
grid problem.
==PAGE:35==
3.1 A Multigrid Linear Solver
23
4. The coarse-grid error is used to correct the ’¼üne-grid approximation, updat-
ing it as vh ŌåÉvh + Ih
HeH. The operator Ih
H is known as the prolongation
operator, transferring the error from the coarse-grid problem to the ’¼üne-
grid problem. This is known as a coarse-grid correction. More explanation
on how it works can be found in section 3.3.
5. An iterative method, such as Gauss-Seidel or Jacobi relaxation, is applied
to a ’¼üne-grid problem. This method must have the property that after
relation the error will be smooth, and smaller than it was before this
process began. Multiple applications, each called a sweep, may be needed.
3.1.1
The Multigrid Idea
The multigrid idea, based on the two-grid idea, is essentially a recursive appli-
cation of the two-grid process as described in the previous section. When the
coarse-grid problem needs to be solved, here given as point 3, this coarse-grid
residual equation can itself be solved by the two-grid process.
In other words, the multigrid idea operates on a set of L grids G1, G2, G3, ..., GL
that all approximate the same domain Ōä”. These grids have di’¼Ćerent amounts
of grid points, with G1 having the most grid points, i.e. being the ’¼ünest grid,
and GL having the least, i.e. being the coarsest grid. The multigrid idea is
simply the application of the 2-grid idea recursively. Such a set of grids can
be seen on ’¼üg. 3.1. Given the assumption that grids are uniform, one could

For the coarsest grid, the problem cannot be solved by another recursive call to
the multigrid process, as there is no coarser grid to restrict to. Instead, it has
to be solved some other way. There are many ways to solve this linear system,
and because the grid is coarse, it is likely that solving it will be cheap.
This linear multigrid idea is purely introduced to improve understanding of
coming ideas. It is not explored further by the report. It is also not implemented
anywhere.

algorithm 2.

3.2
Error Smoothing
Recall that the multigrid process relies on representing the same problem on
multiple grids. According to TrottenbergŌĆÖs Multigrid[TOS00], this is how it gets
its name. Some grids have fewer points than others. In other words they are
coarser. For a coarser grid, since the discretization of the problem is con’¼üned to
the grid, anywhere there is not a grid point, there is also no direct information
on any of the quantities that are de’¼üned or are obtained on the grid. Thus,
when transferring the problem from the ’¼üne grid to the coarse grid, data about
the residual is going to be lost. As Brandt [Bra84] notes, the job of a smoother
is to reduce the importance of the data lost in this process.
It is probably easiest to introduce error smoothing with a concrete example of
a method that will cause error smoothing itself. The smoothing principle, as
given by Trottenberg [TOS00], says that
"Many classical iterative methods (Gauss-Seidel etc.) if appro-
priately applied to discrete elliptic problems have a strong smoothing
e’¼Ćect on the error of any approximation."
This quote speci’¼ücally mentions the Gauss-Seidel iteration, but perhaps it would
be easier to introduce another classical iterative method ’¼ürst: The Jacobi Iter-
ation.
==PAGE:38==
26
Full Approximation Scheme
The model problem introduced in section 1.3 is a discrete elliptic problem. How-
ever, it is not linear unless ╬│ = 0. As this idea is being introduced for a linear
multigrid solver, assume that this is the case. That gives rise to the classical
2-dimensional Poisson equation, which, according to the Encyclopedia of Math-
ematics by Hazewinkel[Haz01], is also an elliptic partial di’¼Ćerential equation.
In addition it has been discretized. So, according to the smoothing principle,
Jacobi iterations will work for this problem to make the error e = uŌłÆv smooth
after some number of applications.
Assume, once again, that the analytical solution to the problem eq. (1.4) is
u(x, y) = (x ŌłÆx2)(y ŌłÆy2)
(3.2)
The right hand side is now:
f(x, y) = 2((x ŌłÆx2)(y ŌłÆy2))
(3.3)
Recall that the error was e = u ŌłÆv. If one de’¼ünes initial guess v to be some
approximation to the exact solution with some noise Žē, one obtains:
v(x, y) = u(x, y) ŌłÆŽē
(3.4)
So the error e, then, is some unknown noise signal. By applying the smoothing
method, one can study how well it eliminates, and more importantly how fast
is smoothes, the error.
Discretizing the problem using a central ’¼ünite di’¼Ćerence approximation leads
to:

3.2.3
Chebyshev Polynomial Smoothing
The Chebyshev iteration is an iterative method for the solution of a system of
linear equations, quite like both Jacobi-iterations and Gauss-Seidel iterations.
Unlike Jacobi-iterations or Gauss-Seidel iterations, it is not a stationary method,
however it does not require the use of inner products or other reduction opera-
tors, as stated by Ghysels et al [GKV11].
However, the Chebyshev iteration requires some information about the spectrum
of the Jacobian matrix J.
More precisely, for a symmetric, positive de’¼ünite
matrix it requires an estimation of the largest Eigenvalue ╬╗max and an estimation
of the smallest Eigenvalue ╬╗min.
Ghysels et al [GKV11] mentions that, for the linear case of the linear elliptic
model problem 1.9, these are known analytically to be ╬╗max =
8
h2 cos2(
ŽĆ
2(NŌłÆ1))
and ╬╗min =
8
h2 sin2(
ŽĆ
2(NŌłÆ1)) where N + 1 is the number of grid points along
each spatial direction in the discretization.
According to Ghysels et al [GKV11], the Chebyshev iteration needs this in order
to compute the center of the ellipse containing the Eigenvalues of J. The center
of the ellipse is denoted d > 0 and the foci are located at d ┬▒ c. They can be
computed from ╬╗max and ╬╗min the following way:
d = ╬╗max + ╬╗min
2
and
c = ╬╗max ŌłÆ╬╗min
2
(3.12)
Now, given the two parameters c and d, [CGR92] de’¼ünes the Chebyshev iteration
as follows: Let u0 be the initial approximate solution, let r0 = f ŌłÆJu0 and

The result of this smoothing can be seen on ’¼üg. 3.4. However, while the method
does indeed solve the problem with remarkably few iterations compared to
Jacobi-Newton or Gauss-Seidel Newton, as a smoother it seems to actually
make the result less smooth than it was to begin with! However, as it turns out,
this can be remedied by overestimating the minimum Eigenvalue. Here are some
examples of simply setting ╬╗min = ╬╗max/5 (’¼üg. 3.5), ╬╗min = ╬╗max/10 (’¼üg. 3.6),
and ╬╗min = ╬╗max/30 (’¼üg. 3.7).
It is clear from this that a more accurate
estimate of ╬╗min leads to a faster solution method, but a deliberately too large
estimate of ╬╗min leads to a method that performs smoothing faster. This also
has the added advantage of this method no longer needing an estimation of the
smallest Eigenvalue!
Since the primary purpose of Chebyshev smoothing is smoothing, it is preferable
that as few smoothing steps will have to be performed as possible. The quicker
the smoothing happens, the better. It is not computationally useful to solve the
coarse grid completely in a Multigrid context, as explained in section 3.3. For
this reason, the estimate ╬╗min = ╬╗max/5 is used for the rest of the report.

Thusly, a linearization must be performed before the ’¼ürst Chebyshev iteration.
The Chebyshev iterations must then attempt to solve the linearized problem,
which results in a correction which can then be used to correct the approximation
to u.
The simplest way to do this is to ’¼ürst retrieve the residual of the non-linear
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Error Smoothing
37
equation. This can be done simply by calling rnonlin = f ŌłÆF(vk) ’¼ürst thing
and computing the Jacobian J from that.
Now, the linear set of equations Je = rnonlin has to be solved. The initial guess
for e is all zeroes.
Because of the non-linear element being added along the diagonal of J in the
linearization, the Eigenvalues are going to be di’¼Ćerent than for the linear case.
However, recalling that Chebyshev smoothing only requires an upper and lower
bound for the Eigenvalues of J, denoted ╬╗max and ╬╗min, one may use WeylŌĆÖs
Inequality, described by Franklin in [Fra93], to estimate these.
Consider the Jacobian matrix broken up into the linear component H and non-
linear component P such that J = H + P.
H has the Eigenvalues associated with the linear problem, and they were given
as ╬Įmax =

╬Įmin + Žümin Ōēż╬╗min

and the same goes for ╬Įmax + Žümax being a fairly good upper bound for ╬╗max.
This satis’¼ües the requirements of the Chebyshev smoother! There are many
more complicated methods of estimating the Eigenvalues out there according to
Ghysels et al [GKV11], but this one is very simple and very easy to compute.
It can become even easier by only estimating the maximum Eigenvalue, and
simply assuming that ╬╗min Ōēł╬╗max/5, as has been done in the tests in this
report. Furthermore, the Maximum Eigenvalue can then be estimated using
Modi’¼üed Power Iterations or the Arnoldi Method, but this is not explored in
this report. This is also mentioned by Ghysels et al [GKV11], but not done
there either.

3.2.5
Conjugate Gradient Method Smoothing
Narushima [Nar13] describes the Conjugate Gradient Method as a Krylov Sub-
space iterative method for the solution of a system of linear equations, quite
==PAGE:51==
3.2 Error Smoothing
39
like the other methods already presented. It turns out that the Conjugate Gra-
dient Method, in addition to being a very e’¼ācient way of solving linear (or
Newton-linearized, as in this case) equations where the system matrix is pos-
itive de’¼ünite, also causes smoothing when applied to non-smooth equations,
including such things as discretizations of elliptic model problems with added
random noise.
The Conjugate Gradient Method requires that the system matrix is symmetric
and positive de’¼ünite. As explained in section 2.2 that is the case with the model
problem when the method is applied to the linear system of equations
Ju = f
(3.17)

is positive de’¼ünite, the solution u will also be the minimizer to the quadratic
equation
f(u) = 1

(3.18)
This means that, if f(uk+1) < f(uk), then Ōłźuk+1 ŌłÆuŌłź< Ōłźuk ŌłÆuŌłź.
This
suggests taking the ’¼ürst basis vector p0 as the negative of gradient of f(u),
which is p0 = f ŌłÆJu0. The other vectors in the basis will be conjugate to the
gradient, which Avriel [Avr03] claims is where the method gets its name.
Let the residual at the kŌĆÖth step now be:
rk = f ŌłÆJuk
(3.19)
Note that rk is the negative gradient of f at uk. It is now required that the
directions pk be conjugate to each other, and that the next search direction
pk+1 be built out of the current residual and all previous search directions.
This gives rise to the following expression:

The result of this smoothing can be seen on ’¼üg. 3.8. It is immediately apparent
that this smoother works incredibly well; the error term appears smooth already
after 2 iterations.
This method acts on the global linearization given by the Multigrid cycle. That
is, the values of J may change between calls to the Conjugate Gradient Method,
but it does not change between iterations.
Truncated Neumann Smoothing was also implemented and brie’¼éy considered.
It did not make it into the testing phase of this report, however, and is therefore
not presented here. For information, go to Appendix B.
3.3
Coarse Grid Correction
Now that a method has been presented which smoothes the error, even if it was
originally random noise, the other important aspect of Multigrid can be brought
up: A quantity that is smooth on a given grid can, without any essential loss
of information, be approximated on a coarser grid. This is because the error
on any point removed can be well approximated by simply assuming it is the
average (or some other interpolation) of the surrounding points.
==PAGE:53==
3.3 Coarse Grid Correction

In TrottenbergŌĆÖs Multigrid [TOS00], this is known as the Coarse Grid Principle.
"A smooth error term is well approximated on a coarse grid. A
coarse grid procedure is substantially less expensive (substantially
fewer grid points) than a ’¼üne grid procedure."
The principle works on the error quantity, which is also called the correction
quantity because it is used to correct the approximation to the true solution.
So the goal here is the correct the approximation using the coarse grid solution
but, because the ’¼üner grid had a smooth error, that error is well represented
as the coarse grid error. What this means is that the coarse grid error can be
used to correct the approximation on the ’¼üne grid fairly well, even for points
for which the correction was not computed for. Doing so is called coarse grid
correction, and it is the essential idea in Multigrid that makes it fast.
But how does one know when the error is smooth enough to perform coarse
grid correction? Well, one way to work it out is to observe plots of a large error
being "smoothed away" by applications of the smoother, and then seeing when
they "appear smooth", as was done in the previous chapter.
A more theoretical understanding of the smoothing needed to perform coarse
==PAGE:54==
42
Full Approximation Scheme
grid correction can be found in appendix C.
3.4
The Full Approximation Scheme (FAS)
The above multigrid method cannot be used to solve non-linear problems, as the
problem itself is given as a set of linear equations represented through matrices.
However, Henson et al [Hen15] points out that it is natural to expect that
Multigrid ideas should be e’¼Ćective for non-linear problems, as there are many
other methods, some quite old, that solves a sequence of linear problems each
based on a linearization of the non-linear problem.
Consider a system of non-linear equations F(u) = f, where F : RN ŌåÆRN, N Ōłł
Z. Once again, the error is given by e = u ŌłÆv.
The residual is now r = f ŌłÆF(v). Subtracting the original equation from the
residual leads to the residual equation for non-linear problems:
F(u) ŌłÆF(v) = r
(3.22)
Since F is non-linear, in general F(e) ╠Ė= r. This means the error cannot be
determined by solving the linear equation on the coarse grid, as is standard in
Multigrid methods. Instead, the residual equation must be used.
Taking another look at the residual equation 3.22, it seems possible to use that to
determine a coarse-grid correction. Say an approximation vh has been found or
is given to the original ’¼üne-grid problem given by the set of non-linear equations
F(u) = f, where F : RN ŌåÆRN, N ŌłłZ, such that uh = vh + eh:
F h(uh) = f h
(3.23)
Equation (3.22) applied to the coarse-grid problem is:
F H(uH) ŌłÆF H(vH) = F H(vH + eH) ŌłÆF H(vH) = rH
(3.24)
And likewise for the ’¼üne-grid problem, it is:
F h(uh) ŌłÆF h(vh) = F h(vh + eh) ŌłÆF H(vh) = rh
(3.25)
Let the coarse-grid residual be the restriction of the ’¼üne-grid residual, and the
’¼üne-grid approximation the restriction of the coarse-grid residual, i.e. rH =
IH
h rh and vH = IH
h vh. This yields:
rH = IH
h rh = IH
h (f h ŌłÆF h(vh))
(3.26)
vH = IH
h vh
(3.27)
==PAGE:55==
3.4 The Full Approximation Scheme (FAS)
43
Using these substitutions in the coarse-grid residual equation yields:
F H(uH) = F H(IH
h vh + eH) = F H(IH
h vh) + IH
h (f h ŌłÆF h(vh)) = f H
(3.28)
Since all quantities for computing f H = F H(IH
h vh) + IH
h (f h ŌłÆF h(vh)) =
F H(vH) + rH are known, the coarse-grid error can be found from the solu-
tion by eH = uH ŌłÆIH
h vh, assuming uH can be found by solving F H(uH) =
F H(vH) + rH.
This coarse-grid error can then be interpolated to the ’¼üne grid and used to
correct the ’¼üne-grid approximation via the update:
vh ŌåÉvh + Ih
H(uH ŌłÆIH
h vh)
(3.29)
According to Henson et al [Hen15], this idea is where the Full Approximation
Scheme gets its name: Because the coarse-grid problem is solved for the full
approximation, rather than the error eH.
Given two grids, the FAS process can be described as follows:
1. An iterative relaxation method, such as Gauss-Seidel or Jacobi, is applied
to a ’¼üne-grid problem. This method must have the property that after
relaxation the error will be smooth. Multiple applications, each called a
sweep, may be needed.
2. Because the error is smooth, it can su’¼āciently accurately be represented
on a coarser grid. This is because the points that are removed are ap-
proximately the same as an interpolation of the surrounding points could
lead to. This allows what is known as coarse-grid correction. Compute the
’¼üne-grid residual rh and approximation vh, and restrict them to the coarse
grid using a restriction operator IH
h . That is, rH = IH
h rh and vH = IH
h vh.
3. Because the coarse grid has fewer grid points, the coarse grid problem is
less expensive to solve than the ’¼üne grid problem. Note now the coarse-
grid problem F H(uH) = F H(vH) + rH. Because F H(vH) + rH is known,
uH can be found by solving it. This allows ’¼ünding the coarse-grid approx-
imation to the error: eH = uH ŌłÆvH.
4. The coarse-grid error is used to correct the ’¼üne-grid approximation, up-
dating it as vh ŌåÉvh + Ih
H(uH ŌłÆIH
h vh). The operator Ih
H is known as the
prolongation operator, transferring the error from the corase-grid problem
to the ’¼üne-grid problem.
5. An iterative method, such as Gauss-Seidel or Jacobi relaxation, is applied
to a ’¼üne-grid problem. This method must have the property that after
relation the error will be smooth, and smaller than it was before this
process began. Multiple applications, each called a sweep, may be needed.
==PAGE:56==
44
Full Approximation Scheme
Brandt [Bra84] describes FAS a generalization of the coarse-grid correction
scheme to non-linear problems.
In addition, similar to the linear Multigrid
algorithm, solving the coarse-grid approximation can itself be a FAS process,
wherein the old coarse grid is the new ’¼üne grid, and an even more coarse grid
is de’¼üned.
Just like NewtonŌĆÖs Method, FAS is an iterative method. At each step, the ’¼üne
grid is corrected, and convergence is checked via the same criteria as NewtonŌĆÖs
Method, given in the list 2.1. If the criteria are not ful’¼ülled, a new iteration of
the FAS process begins and k is incremented by one.
FAS can also be written algorithmically, as shown in algorithm 9 and algo-
rithm 10.

3.4.1

Several times in the immediately previous section, there have been mentions of
having the solve the coarse-grid problem. For linear multigrid, the problem was
JHeH = rH. For FAS, it was F H(uH) = F H(vH) + rH.
It was mentioned that the problem could be solved either directly, or through
recursively doing the FAS process again by rede’¼üning the current coarse grid as

the ’¼üne grid, and constructing an even more coarse grid. Once that was done,
one could return to the original problem and ’¼ünish its FAS process.
Since each FAS process had a restriction before the recursive call and a pro-
longation after it, choosing to recursively use the FAS process in this manner
L times would result ’¼ürst in L ŌłÆ1 restrictions and then L ŌłÆ1 prolongations.
This is known as V-cycling. If L > 2, Brandt [Bra84] notes that is a multilevel
multigrid method.
In general, the choice of when to restrict or prolongate to a di’¼Ćerent grid is
called cycling.
In this section, there will be a plot for each type of cycling. On each plot, the
sequence of grids on which the problem is transferred is indicated along the y-
axis, with the ’¼ünest grid at the top and the coarsest grid on the bottom. Recall
that there are L grids G1, G2, G3, ..., GL that all approximate the same domain
Ōä”, and that G1 is the ’¼ünest grid and GL is the coarsest. On all the plots 4 levels
are shown, i.e. L = 4. V-cycling gets its name for how the cycle appears on
such a plot, which is shown on ’¼üg. 3.9.
Only V-cycling is explored in any depth in this report, but there are several
other types that deserve a brief mention.
==PAGE:58==
46
Full Approximation Scheme
Index i of Gi
1
2
3
4
Figure 3.9: V-cycling for the Full Approximation Scheme with 4 levels of grids
One other type of cycle is the W-cycle.
The W-cycle is de’¼üned for at least
3 grids.
At the beginning, it restricts as far as possible, and at the end it
prolongates as far as possible. Exactly in the middle between these two, the
W-cycle prolongates to the 2nd ’¼ünest grid, then restricted to the coarsest grid
again, and then ’¼ünally prolongates all the way back to the ’¼ünest grid.
Between the 2nd ’¼ünest grid and the two other cases of the 2nd ’¼ünest grid to
either side, there are prolongations followed by restrictions to the 3rd ’¼ünest grid.
This idea also runs for each of the peaks of 3rd ’¼ünest grids to their respective
cases of 3rd ’¼ünest grid to either side of them, each of these cases having peaks
of the 4th ’¼ünest grid between them. And so on it goes. W-cycling gets its name
for the same reason V-cycling gets itsŌĆÖ name, and is shown on ’¼üg. 3.10.
The ’¼ünal important scheme to mention is the full multigrid scheme or FMG. It
can be useful for obtaining a good initial guess to the ’¼üne-grid problem while
starting with only an approximation of the solution to the problem on the coars-
est grid, which is useful as a starting point for a V-cycling FAS.
FMG starts on the coarsest grid, then solves the coarsest problem directly. After
this, it prolongates the grid, then immediately restricts it again to the coarsest
grid and solves the problem directly. Then it prolongates twice and restricts
twice, and solves the problem again. It keeps going like this, adding one extra
prolongation and restriction every time, until it reaches the ’¼ünest grid once.
Then it does a full V-cycle, and ’¼ünishes with a ’¼üne-grid approximation of the
result. From this point, further V-cycling can be done as desired. FMG-cycling
is shown on ’¼üg. 3.11.

3.5.3
Solving at the Coarsest Grid
There are a huge number of ways to do this. Broadly speaking, they can be
separated into two categories.
The ’¼ürst of these is obviously solving the (coarse) linear problem as accurately
as possible using direct solution methods, such as LU-factorization. This gives
a good coarse-grid approximation of ╦£u, which solves the linearized problem and
approximates u.
Because the grid is very coarse, it is also computationally
cheap.
The other method is to use the smoother to make sure the coarsest grid has a
smooth error, and then rely on coarse grid correction to correct for the smooth
error, which after all can be well represented on this coarsest grid.
Generally, there is no good reason to solve the coarse grid problem to any real
accuracy. As long as the resulting correction is smooth, coarse-grid correction
should take care of the rest. For this reason, direct solvers, being more expensive
(compare section 2.4 with section 3.7 are not desirable, and will not be explored
further.
Trottenberg [TOS00] points out that successive post-smoothing at every grid
level, which is necessary anyway, means that the coarse-grid correction achieves
greater accuracy at the ’¼üner grids in spite of the fact that the approximation
==PAGE:62==
50
Full Approximation Scheme
to the solution of the linearised problem at the coarsest grid was somewhat
inaccurate.
3.6
Convergence
Trottenberg [TOS00] argues that, in order to be able to construct, evaluate and
analyze any Multigrid method in practice, one wants to determine its average
convergence factor, Žü, by measurement.
Generally, the only quantities that are always available for any given problem,
regardless of whether any analytical solution is known or not, is the residual at
any iteration k at the ’¼ünest level with grid spacing h: rk,h.
Trottenberg [TOS00] calculates it as:
Žü =
m
s
|rm,h|
|r0,h|
(3.32)
for some appropriate norm. Here, m is the total number of iterations taken, such
than an average of the convergence factor for the entire process is obtained. That
is, FAS is expected to have linear convergence:
Ōłźek+1Ōłź= ŽüŌłźekŌłź
(3.33)
Concrete proofs do exist about the convergence of the Full Approximation
Scheme with V-cycles, but typically there are a great deal of assumptions in-
volved.
Many of these assumptions do not hold in practice.
For instance,
Reusken [Reu88] provides an excellent proof of this, but it assumes that only
a single pre-smoothing and a single post-smoothing step is taken at each level
and that this smoothing allows the coarse grid correction to operate optimally,
but also that it only smoothes precisely enough to not begin approximating
the solution by itself. These assumptions are almost certainly not correct - but
whether they are will be tested in section 4.4.1.
This point will be illustrated by noting that, theoretically, the convergence of
FAS in terms of iterations is supposed to be independent of the grid spacing h
at the ’¼ünest grid.
Ultimately, developing the best multigrid method, or indeed FAS method, is
all about testing various components for it and using eq. (3.32) to ’¼ünd the
convergence factor and ’¼ünding the best option of the lot.

3.7 Computational Complexity

In this case, that means comparing the convergence rate and computational
requirements of FAS for various amounts of non-linearity with various di’¼Ćerent
smoothers presented in this chapter. This, and many more tests, is carried out
in section 4.4.
3.7
Computational Complexity
The computational complexity of the Full Approximation Scheme can be derived
from the fact that the Full Approximation Scheme is a generalized multigrid
method for non-linear problems.
An advantage to Multigrid often cited by proponents of the method, such as
Brandt [Bra84], is that its computational cost scales linearly with the number
of unknowns.
Given a di’¼Ćerential equation which can be solved approximately on a grid Gk,
assume that the solution can be obtained with the work Wk = ŽüKnk from a
solution on the coarse grid k + 1. Here nk is the number of grid points on the
grid Gk and K is the constant amount of computation applied for every grid
point from the smoothing, coarse grid correction, function evaluation, etc., and
Žü = nk+1/nk < 1.
Žü is assumed to be constant for any two grids in the hierarchy, because the same
restriction and prolongation operators are used. Furthermore, K is assumed to
be constant as well, with the same amount of smoothing operations applied to
every iteration. This assumption implies that the number of smoothing sweeps
does not change for every iteration (which, in the following implementation, it
doesnŌĆÖt).
This leads to the following recurrence relation for obtaining the solution on the
grid Gk:
Wk = Wk+1 + ŽüKnk
(3.34)
In particular, for the ’¼ünest grid, i.e. when k = 1, this must be the case: W1 =
W2 + ŽüKn1. Combining these two expressions and noting that nk = ŽükŌłÆ1n1
gives:
W1 = Kn1

(3.35)
This geometric series can also be written (easily provable by induction) as:
W1 < Kn1

==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4
Sequential Solvers
NewtonŌĆÖs Method and the Full Approximation Scheme (FAS) described above
had to implemented on a language that is fast and in which parallelism was
possible. C was chosen for this purpose, as it is fast and parallellism is possible
through OpenMP for shared memory systems. Shared memory systems will be
considered in this report, primarily in chapter 5.
For this chapter, focus is on getting an implementation of the ideas presented
in chapter 2 and chapter 3 that is sequential, i.e. with no parallelism whatso-
ever and no attempts to exploit the modern many-core architectures. This will
make it easier to create a version of the software which works on many-core
architectures later on.
4.1
Choice of libraries and other development soft-
ware
The ’¼ürst thing anyone would need for implementing a C program and testing it
is, obviously, a C compiler, debugger, and pro’¼üler. For this purpose, the GNU C
Compiler version 4.9, GNU Debugger, and Valgrind were used. These software
packages work very well with Linux, and as such Linux was used as well. The
==PAGE:66==
54
Sequential Solvers
build system chosen was GNU make. A make’¼üle for the project can be found
in Appendix D.1.5.
As explained in chapter 2, matrix and vector operations are very common in
both FAS and Newton methods. This includes vector to vector operations such
as dot products, norms, reductions, and so on. It also includes multiplication
of matrix and vector. The use of matrix-matrix operations, however, is limited
- in some cases there is none of it at all. Nevertheless, the Basic Linear Algebra
Software (BLAS) is very useful here.
Various implementations exist of this.
For this implementation OpenBLAS was chosen. Its documentation [QXYY13]
states that not only is it well optimized for a single thread, it can also be built
with OpenMP, and is well optimized for shared memory systems. This turns
out to be a boon later on in this project.
Additionally, NewtonŌĆÖs Method also requires the ability to solve potentially large
linear systems.
For this purpose, thereŌĆÖs the Linear Algebra PACKage, also
known as LAPACK. LAPACK contains, among many other things, solution
methods for such problems using LU factorization. Conveniently, OpenBLAS
also contains a full LAPACK implementation optimized for shared memory ar-
chitectures using OpenMP.
Given that row-major is the native matrix ordering in C, row-major was chosen
for the implementation. Because of this, BLAS and LAPACK, being imple-
mented in FORTRAN and therefore using column major ordering, were prob-
lematic. This was solved using the wrappers known as Cblas and LAPACKe
for BLAS and LAPACK respectively. Both of these wrappers are also built into
OpenBLAS, [QXYY13].
For extremely large linear systems, it can very well happen that constructing the
entire Jacobian matrix for NewtonŌĆÖs Method becomes extremely expensive - not
just in terms of CPU cycles, but certainly also in terms of memory consumption.
This is because when doubling the grid size for a 2-dimensional elliptic non-linear
boundary problem along each spatial direction, N, the number of unknowns
increases by approximately N 2. The Jacobian, having to de’¼üne the derivative
for every variable at every point, increases by the square of the amount of points,
so N 4. Besides giving a good baseline for discussing weak scaling in section 5.4,
this also makes it impossible to work with large grids, such as 2048x2048, even
if the compute performance was present, as memory becomes an issue.
However, in the vast majority of cases, especially for large matrices, these deriva-
tives result in 0, as explained in section 2.2. This means that the very large
Jacobian matrix can be represented with a sparse format. While BLAS and
LAPACK does have some support for sparse systems built in, it is far easier to
deal with an API which allows one to work with sparse systems directly, and
==PAGE:67==
4.2 Sequential NewtonŌĆÖs Method Implementation
55
which also allows solving large linear systems using LU-factorization. Conve-
niently, there is a software package which does exactly that, and its name is
SuperLU. It is documented by Xiaoye S. Li [Li05].
This concludes the list of software packages used in the implementation of the
software. A detailed instruction on how to install this setup, as well as which
system it was installed on for the tests, can be found in Appendix A.
Throughout this implementation e’¼Ćort there is separation between what is part
of the problem itself and what is part of the solution method. To that end, the
implementation is split into 3 main ’¼üles:
ŌĆó One for the solution methods themselves, as seen in Appendix D.1.2.
ŌĆó One for the elliptic non-linear boundary problem and its related functions,
as seen in Appendix D.1.3.
ŌĆó One for miscellenarious useful functions, such as ’¼ünding the largest or
smallest value in a vector, constructing an identity matrix, and other re-
lated things, as seen in Appendix D.1.1.
A little trick here is that every solver function implemented, including functions
called by the solvers, are always given a void pointer p at the end of the function
header. This allows passing around various constants and other useful things
through the solver and into functions that the solver calls.
4.2
Sequential NewtonŌĆÖs Method Implementation
NewtonŌĆÖs Method was described in section 2.1. In this case, it is implemented
speci’¼ücally for solving the problem
F(u) = f ŌćÆf ŌłÆF(u) = 0
(4.1)
where r is the residual that arises from f ŌłÆF(v) = r, where v is some approxi-
mation of u with the error e = u ŌłÆv.
The solver will need an initial guess v0 as well as the size of this array. It takes
that in the form of assuming it is dealing with a square domain in ndims with
an array dimsizes of length ndims that tells how many unknowns there are
along each spatial dimension. The total number of points on the grid n, and
consequently the total number of unknowns, is the product of all dimsizes.
==PAGE:68==
56
Sequential Solvers
This result, n, denotes the length of f, r and a workspace LAPACK needs
for pivoting. The Jacobian J is the square of that size, n2. This memory is
immediately allocated and will be freed only when the function exits for any
reason.
The user may like to set a few options to the solver for what the tolerances of
error are before the method is stopped. This is entered as an array of doubles.
In addition, NewtonŌĆÖs Method needs access to the problems themselves. These
are given as functions. The headers of those functions must be on the form:
1
void (*fun)(double *x, double *f, double *r, double *J, size_t *dimsizes,
size_t ndims, void *p)
2
void (*rhs)(double *f, size_t *dimsizes, size_t ndims, void *p)
The ’¼ürst function computes the residual and Jacobian of the residual as ex-
plained in section 2.2. It does so at the start of every iteration. The right hand
side, given in section 2.2 and denoted f, is computed once before the ’¼ürst iter-
ation. For the ’¼ürst function called fun, x, f, dimsizes, ndims, and p are inputs.
r and J are outputs. Note that they are passed by reference. For the second
function called rhs, f is the output, and everything else is input.
There are two implementations of NewtonŌĆÖs Method.
The ’¼ürst one works with a dense Jacobian matrix and solves the globally lin-
earized problem to take the Newton Step at every iteration using LAPACKŌĆÖs
dgesv function, which the documentation [QXYY13] claims implements solving
linear systems using LU-factorization.
The second one works with a sparse Jacobian Matrix de’¼üned using compressed
row storage (CRS) and then solves the globally linearized problem to take the
Newton Step at every iteration using SuperLUŌĆÖs dgssv function, which imple-
ments solving sparse linear systems using LU-factorization.
Should the process of solving the linear system fail for any reason, the implemen-
tation terminates and reports an error. The Newton Step is then taken using
the BLAS function daxpy. The norm is taken with the BLAS function dnrm2.
More information on these can be found in LAPACKŌĆÖs userŌĆÖs guide [ABB+99]
and the documentation for OpenBLAS [QXYY13].
De’¼üning the problem functions themselves, previously called fun and rhs, is
up next.
For the elliptic non-linear boundary problem, the true solution is
already known. The right hand side, which is implemented as rhs has to be
==PAGE:69==
4.3 Sequential FAS Implementation
57
constructed by inserting the true solution into the left hand side of the equation.
Equation (1.10) describes exactly how this is done.
As for the Jacobian, it is constructed exactly as described in 2.2. There are two
implementations of this as well. One for a full Jacobian matrix for use with
LAPACK, and one for sparse compressed row storage matrices for use with
SuperLU.
A complete code listing, including the implementation of NewtonŌĆÖs Method, can
be found in Appendix D.1.2 and the implementation of the elliptic non-linear
boundary problem for use in NewtonŌĆÖs Method can be found in Appendix D.1.3.
4.3
Sequential FAS Implementation
The Full Approximation Scheme was described in chapter 3. In this case, it is
implemented speci’¼ücally for solving the problem:
F(u) = f
(4.2)
For FAS, one not only needs to be able to compute the residual. It is also vital
that the right hand side of eq. (4.2) that has to be solved can be computed, and
the same applies to the left hand side, as seen in chapter 3.
There are several ways to accomplish this in code.
One way is to de’¼üne a
function that gives the left hand side and one that gives the right hand side,
and then simply de’¼üning the function that gives the residual as the di’¼Ćerence
between the two.
The FAS and FASCycle functions have a large number of inputs, including
tons of function pointers. Problem-speci’¼üc implementations of all of the above
functions as well as restriction and prolongation operators, and a coarse grid
solver, a function to compute the grid sizes at every iteration are needed by FAS.
The FASCycle also needs to keep track of the level on which it is operating. The
header ends up looking like this:

So you need to supply a way to compute the residual (which can in turn call
the following two functions, for simplicity), the left hand side, the right hand
side. You also need to supply a smoothing method, a restriction method, a
prolongation method, a method to compute the grid size, an initial estimate of
the grid, the grid spacing on the ’¼ünest grid, stopping criteria, number of grid
levels, and ’¼ünally the size of the ’¼ünest grid (which can also hold coarser grid
sizes as needed).
FAS itself is an iterative method that can have the same stopping criteria as
NewtonŌĆÖs Method, as noted in chapter 3. Thus, the body of the FAS solver is
nearly identical to the one for NewtonŌĆÖs Method, as described in section 4.2.
However, the FAS cycle is used in place of a direct solution method.
The standard 2D prolongation operator was used in the FAS cycle, and it was
implemented in accordance with the description in section 3.5.2 as a simple
loop over all the elements in the ’¼üne grid. The standard 2D restriction operator
was used, and it was also implemented in accordance with the description in
section 3.5.1 as a simple loop over all elements on the coarse grid.
Non-linear versions of the 4 smoothers described in 3.2. Gauss-Seidel and Ja-
cobi smoothers are well known, but a new type of smoother using Chebyshev
Polynomials has also been implemented in a matrix-free way - in other words
the Jacobian is never de’¼üned fully and kept in memory, but knowledge of its
structure is exploited for the smoother. The same is done for the Conjugate
Gradient Method.
This is otherwise known as implementing them "matrix-free".
Because the
structure of the Jacobian matrix is known, in accordance with section 2.2, which
parts of the matrix are multiplied onto which parts of the vector it is multiplied
with is easy to predict. This is very much the same trick that was employed when
==PAGE:71==
4.3 Sequential FAS Implementation
59
working out how to write the Jacobi and Gauss-Seidel smoothers element-wise
in eq. (3.8) and eq. (3.11). More on this in section 4.3.1.
OpenBLAS was used often throughout the implementation, particularly for
vector-vector operations.
A complete code listing, including the implementation of the Full Approximation
Scheme Multigrid Method, can be found in Appendix D.1.2 and the implemen-
tation of the elliptic non-linear boundary problem for use in FAS can be found
in Appendix D.1.3.
4.3.1
Memory considerations
As discussed in chapter 3, FAS consists of a number of components. The stan-
dard prolongation and restriction operator shown in said chapter does not need
the Jacobian to even be known, nor does computing the right hand side or coarse
grid correction.
However, smoothing does need the Jacobian to be known at each iteration,
because they are all actually preconditioners intended for solving a linear system
that has been repurposed for smoothing. However, in all the cases presented
above, the smoothing can be performed matrix-free by working out beforehand
how each element in the solution vector will be changed when multiplied by the
Jacobian.
In practice for this problem, that will generally look like this for J(uk)Ōłåk:
J(u)Ōłå= (4hŌłÆ2+╬│(eui,j+ui,jeui,j))Ōłåi,jŌłÆhŌłÆ2(ŌłåiŌłÆ1,j+Ōłåi+1,j+Ōłåi,jŌłÆ1+Ōłåi,j+1)
(4.3)
Additional memory can be saved by not allocating the coarse or ’¼üne grid correc-
tion quantities as a separate variable. Immediately after calling the FAS cycle,
the correction is computed, after which the result of the recursively called FAS
Cycle is no longer needed. After this the correction is prolongated, after which
the coarse grid correction quantity is no longer needed. Finally, the approxima-
tion is updated based on that coarse grid correction quantity, after which it is
no longer needed.
In addition, there is also no need to store separate variables for the residuals,
both for the ’¼üne grid and the coarse grid. Of course, these variables must be
known before the FAS Cycle is called recursively, but before the recursive call
the correction and corrected quantities are not known, so this memory can be
==PAGE:72==
60
Sequential Solvers
used to store the residual until after the recursive FAS Cycle call, at which point
the old residuals are no longer needed.
In other words, there is no need to allocate memory for the correction and
residual quantities - the memory normally occupied by the result can be used
instead.
All these things combined can drastically reduce the amount of memory needed
by the FAS cycle. For every interior point, approximately 5 double precision
’¼éoats and 6 integers are saved from the sparsely allocated Jacobian at every
level. In addition, 2 double precision ’¼éoats are saved for for the error quantity
for every unknown on both the coarse and the ’¼üne grids at every level except
the coarsest.
In total, the FAS solver ends up allocating 3 quantities on each grid point for
every grid level. Any further quantity will only be de’¼üned by the smoother.
Paying close attention to the amount of memory used by FAS, as well as how
much bandwidth is needed, is key to making it run fast. As an O(n) algorithm
the Roo’¼éine model, shown by Williams et al [WWP09], sets that expectation
that it is memory-bound. More generally, there is also an advantage from having
a lean implementation in general.
4.4
Tests
The test section is divided into two parts. One is the Algorithmic E’¼āciency,
and the other is the Computational E’¼āciency.
The Algorithmic E’¼āciency is a measure of how well the method converges, with
comparisons to the theoretical groundwork.
Computational E’¼āciency is a measure of how long it takes the test system used,
which is described in appendix A, to solve the problem in various con’¼ügurations.
Memory usage also plays a role in computational e’¼āciency. This can then be
compared to the theoretical computational cost.
Both solution methods will be tested for a variety of di’¼Ćerent values of ╬│, which
will determine the non-linearity, as well as for a variety of di’¼Ćerent problem
sizes.
ALL tests were carried out 5 times and the average of the results was taken to
==PAGE:73==
4.4 Tests
61
try to eliminate small variations in the speed at which the result is obtained,
usually caused by the CPU usage of other processes on the system.
The methods are tasked with solving the elliptic non-linear boundary problem
given in section 1.3 until the residual is 10ŌłÆ10 times as large as the residual
was before the ’¼ürst iteration taken or when the largest residual for any point
on the grid is smaller than 10ŌłÆ8 for both NewtonŌĆÖs Method and FAS. The code
primarily used to do this can be found in D.1.3, though the input parameters
were subtly changed at each iteration.
The restriction and prolongation operators were chosen as described in sec-
tion 3.5.1 section 3.5.2 respectively.
The 6 methods being tested are:
1. NewtonŌĆÖs Method with LU-factorization carried out on a full Jacobian
Matrix
2. NewtonŌĆÖs Method with LU-factorization carried out on a sparse CSR Ja-
cobian Matrix
3. The Full Approximation Scheme using 200 (more on this later) sweeps
with the Newton-Jacobi smoother. A new global linearisation is carried
with each sweep.
4. The Full Approximation Scheme using 5 sweeps with the Newton-GaussSeidel
smoother. A new global linearisation is carried with each sweep.
5. The Full Approximation Scheme using 5 sweeps with the Chebyshev-
polynomial smoother. Only 1 linearisation is carried out per smoothing.
6. The Full Approximation Scheme using 2 sweeps of the Conjugate Gradient
based smoother. Only 1 linearisation is carried out per smoothing.
The coarse grid solution method used for the Full Approximation Scheme is
always the normal pre-smoothing immediately followed by the normal post-
smoothing. That is, it depends on the smoother chosen, and twice as many
sweeps and linearisations are carried out.
For the Full Approximation Scheme, every test always lets the coarsest grid be
2 ├Ś 2 cells, with ’¼üner grid from that being 4 ├Ś 4, and ’¼üner grid from that being
8 ├Ś 8, and so on. So, for the 8 ├Ś 8 test, the number of levels is 3. For the
8192 ├Ś 8192 test, the number of levels is 13.
==PAGE:74==
62
Sequential Solvers
As these tests were carried out on the system described on appendix A.1, which
has a Haswell processor, there are some compiler optimizations that may be
used. Intel Corporation [Cor14] mention support for Advanced Vector Exten-
sions 2 (AVX2) and FMA, a 256-bit data path for memory operation, AVX
’¼éoating point operations, and high L1D and L2 cache bandwidth. GCC can
take advantage of these features in the form of compiler optimizations using the
march=haswell -O3 compiler ’¼éags, as well as thoughtful but sporadic use of the
"register" and "align" keywords in the code, according to the GNU FoundationŌĆÖs
GCC UserŌĆÖs Guide [Fou15].
Intel Corporation [Cor14] gives a few ideas for optimizing further: The register
keyword is often used for loops and other variables that change extremely often.
It instructs the compiler to try to keep the variables in cache. The align keyword
can be used to tell the computer to align double precision ’¼éoating numbers
within to addresses divisible by some number, and additionally so they each
occupy exactly 64 bits and are spaced 64 bits apart from each other, consistently.
This means that 4 numbers can be computed at in one go using the AVX ’¼éoating
point extension. Additionally, exactly 64 numbers will ’¼üt on a single page on
the processor when it is transported into the cache. Blocking is also performed
via the compiler according to the GNU Foundation[Fou15].
The only extra optimization ’¼éags added besides those, and they do cover a very
wide range of optimizations already, are -floop-interchange -ftree-loop-optimize.
The GNU Foundation [Fou15] mentions these are meant speci’¼ücally for dealing
with cache issues that may arise due to poor nested looping, as well as for op-
timizing out a very intuitively nice use of a boundary condition marker in the
residual evaluation function, but which would otherwise cause some slowness.
All the ’¼éags together are: -std=c11 -D_XOPEN_SOURCE=600 -Wall -O3 -fopenmp
-lm -lopenblas -lsuperlu -march=haswell -floop-interchange -ftree-loop-optimize

for di’¼Ćerent values of ╬│.

Method is concerned, F ŌĆ▓ŌĆ▓(xk) = 0. Since that is the neglected part of the Taylor
polynomial used, the problem is immediately solved to machine accuracy on the
’¼ürst step.

For ╬│ > 0 it does not immediately converge. As expected, when the method is
su’¼āciently close to converging, it will have second order convergence. For ╬│ = 1
and to some extent ╬│ = 10 this is evidently the case.
Also as expected, making the equation more sti’¼Ćby making the non-linear part
of it dominate will make NewtonŌĆÖs Method converge slower, as the size of the
neglected term grows as ╬│ increases. Because this forces the residual to stay
larger, the method will consistently be further away from the root it is seeking.
The Full Approximation Scheme behaves somewhat unintuitively for this prob-
lem. The more non-linear the problem becomes, the faster it converges. This
is not an unusual phenomenon for this problem, however. It was also described
by Christensen et al [lCCE12] and Henson et al [Hen15]. The authors of these
works also noted it in other literature, and cited appropriately.
It is, however, an unusual e’¼Ćect e’¼Ćect for other non-linear problems. It happens
because the non-linear term only involves ui,j and none of its neighbouring
values. So, as ╬│ becomes larger, the problem becomes more local. Put another
way, the diagonal of J begins to dominate the problem. As this occurs, the
relaxation sweeps, which are also local in nature, become more e’¼Ćective.
One of the main objectives of choosing the components for a Full Approximation
Scheme method, is to get the convergence factor Žü as low as possible, as seen
in eq. (3.32). The 4 di’¼Ćerent smoothers mentioned in chapter 3 are tested and
compared for various values of ╬│. The result can be seen in table 4.1 Intuitively,
it appears the the Jacobi method is best for a very non-linear problem, but there

One of the most straight forward tests for computational e’¼āciency is simply to
measure how quickly the method solves the elliptic non-linear boundary problem
to su’¼ācient accuracy for various grid sizes using a CPU wall timer on a single
