much longer than any other method. This is not altogether surprising, since the
computational complexity was expected to be approximately O(n3). Note that
this ’¼ügure shows the CPU wall time as a function of the cells per dimension, N.
The number of unknowns this gives rise to is n = (N + 1)2.
So, an algorithm with O(n3) complexity will show up as O(N 6+2N 2) complexity
- and that is exactly what NewtonŌĆÖs Method does when based on a full LU-
factorization.
The SuperLU-based version of NewtonŌĆÖs Method shows up as
roughly O(N 3) = O(N 1.5) complexity. This is as expected, because, according
to Xiaoye S. Li [Li05], a sparse LU factorization does not need to do anything
to large parts of the matrix, and this can be exploited in the LU-factorization.
The Jacobi-Newton based FAS implementation appears to be strikingly bad
compared to FAS using the other smoothers. This is because the Jacobi Newton
smoother failed to actually smooth the result, and instead ends up solving the
Newton-linearized problem fairly accurately instead. This is exactly the same
thing NewtonŌĆÖs Method does, and for this reason the two implementations are
going to perform very similarly.
FAS with Gauss-Seidel, Chebyshev, and Conjugate Gradient smoothing all show
near O(N 2) complexity - as expected from any multigrid method, including FAS,
using a good smoother. Chebyshev smoothing appears to be slightly slower than
the two others, which are neck-and-neck.
Contrary to the assumption about smoothers made in the proof referenced in
section 3.6, although not contrary to realistic expectation, smoothing generally
does not involve a single sweep that smoothes just well enough but does almost
==PAGE:80==
68
Sequential Solvers
nothing to solve the problem in and of itself. In order work out whether the
computational e’¼āciency in terms of ’¼éoating point operation work matches with
the theoretical expectation, it is useful to compare the amount of work for a
single Newton Step or complete V-cycle for the di’¼Ćerent methods.
The result of carrying out that test can be seen on ’¼üg. 4.6. It is noteworthy how
accurate the predicted runtime was for the working FAS methods. The expec-
tation was also spot-on for NewtonŌĆÖs Method in both cases, although for very
small problem sizes, the work of taking the step itself and setting up function
values, which were both O(N 2) operations, are more visible.
FAS with Jacobi smoothing proves to spend roughly the same amount of time per
iteration as the Sparse NewtonŌĆÖs Method. The problem problem is that it takes a
really large amount of V-cycles to actually solve anything, so the total runtime
is really high.
Comparing it to NewtonŌĆÖs Method for sparse systems, which
actually solves the linearized problem to very high accuracy, it is completely
useless.

When presenting the data, I made sure to add the work performed by the
OpenMP threads (there was only 1 per function, because this is a test of single-
threaded performance, i.e. OMP_NUM_THREADS=1), so that should be accounted
for. However, the ’¼éag -O3 may have optimized some of the functions out entirely,
because it was faster to run them in-line. This can very well be the case with the
norm function, which is just an if-then-else statement that calls BLAS functions
where appropriate. When inserted into the function Norm is called from, the
time of the BLAS function call will then be added to the function Norm was
called from instead.
In addition, the Right-Hand and Gridsize computation were in some cases also
not called.
This is also likely due to an optimization, but this call and the
frequency with which it was called did not change between tests. Why it has
been optimized out in some cases and not in others, I simply cannot explain.
Of course, one could run the pro’¼üling without any compiler optimization, but
that would imply not actually testing the fastest version of the program the
current code can generate, which is somewhat pointless. It is also possible that
the pro’¼üling software itself is at fault, but I have no concrete evidence of this.
The software used was the GNU pro’¼üler gprof, which generated the data then
opened by Eclipse CDT 3.8. Eclipse was not used for this project at all outside
of pro’¼üling.

Sequential Solvers
For the Sparse Newton method, solving the direct system completely dominates
everything else to the point where everything else appears to be using 0% of
the CPU time - except the problem de’¼ünition, because it is busy setting up
the Jacobian. Despite its somewhat decent theoretical complexity, It is very
clearly a much slower method for these types of problems than FAS with a good
smoother is (although it is useful for many other types of problems).
Besides that, the main result of interest is the amount of time spent on smooth-
ing versus the amount of time spent on de’¼üning the problem. The result here
shows that the Gauss-Seidel run spent the largest amount of time on smoothing
- but it also ’¼ünished fastest. This is quite likely because it managed to solve the
problem in fewer iterations, so the residual did not have to be updated as many
times. The Conjugate Gradient method shows exactly the opposite by spending
less time smoothing yet more time computing the result.
As seen on ’¼üg. 4.6, neither of these situations are necessarily an advantage or
a disadvantage.
These two solvers solve the problem di’¼Ćerently, essentially.
One uses more iterations, one spends longer in each iteration. An obvious test
would be to see if this can be improved for the Conjugate Gradient method by
increasing the number of sweeps from 2 to 3 - but this actually increases the
average compute time for this problem from 14.527 to 16.004 seconds over 5 test
runs.
Conclusively, the Conjugate Gradient method needs more work to apply each
sweep, but smoothes su’¼āciently well with fewer sweeps.
The Chebyshev smoother strikes somewhat a balance between the two. It needs
more iterations to carry out its work and thus spends longer time re-evaluating
the residual, and it also takes longer to apply each smoothing sweep and it needs
the same number of sweeps as Gauss-Seidel to be e’¼Ćective. So, for the sequential
case, it is not good. However, it is not so far behind that it is a huge issue -
perhaps better parallelism might solve it? This question will be answered in
chapter 5.
4.4.2.1
Memory Usage
Another noteworthy thing about what is shown on ’¼üg. 4.6 is a lack of results
for large grid sizes for NewtonŌĆÖs Method - both Sparse and Full, and also for
FAS Jacobi. The FAS Jacobi method tests were stopped because, as mentioned
in the preceding chapter, the result wasnŌĆÖt that interesting due to an error, and
the computation time was exploding because so many iterations were needed, as
solving a linear system, nevermind a non-linear system, with the Jacobi method
==PAGE:83==
4.4 Tests
71
is asymptotic, thus overwhelmingly slow.
With NewtonŌĆÖs Method, the issue was slightly di’¼Ćerent: The program crashed,
or closed complaining about being unable to allocate the required memory, or
just becoming incredibly slow.
Figure 4.7 is a test of the peak memory usage for all the 6 available methods.
Recall that the system these tests are carried out on has 16GB of memory, as
listed in appendix A. The maximum resident set size, i.e. the total memory
usage of the program, is taken from the Linux tool time -v.

As mentioned in section 4.2, NewtonŌĆÖs Method has the property of needing the
entire Jacobian to be de’¼üned in memory at once. When using a dense matrix
for the Jacobian, this very quickly ’¼ülls up all available memory; mostly with
zeroes. However, just having to keep the Jacobian in memory at all times, even
in sparse format, requires an enormous amount of memory. It is very clear that
NewtonŌĆÖs Method uses a lot more memory than the FAS methods.
The most e’¼Ćective method, memory wise, appears to be Gauss-Seidel. This is
not surprising, given that this method does not require any additional memory
allocation at all. Jacobi requires one additional allocation.
==PAGE:84==
72
Sequential Solvers
Chebyshev and Conjugate Gradient smoothing both require roughly similar
amounts of memory - approximately 4 extra values at every grid point while
the smoother is running. However, this is still substantially less memory than
NewtonŌĆÖs Method requires.
In FAS of all varieties, more memory is allocated as the solver calls itself recur-
sively for the coarser grids, and is then freed again afterwards, such that only
the ’¼ünest grid is allocated at all times and between individual V-cycles. Fig-
ure 4.8 shows what that can look like, and this is generally the case for all FAS
methods. In every case tested, peak memory was achieved almost immediately,
so if the problem cannot be solved with the available memory, the user will know
very quickly, which is nice. This graph is going to show lower bounds, as only
the heap memory is counted using the program Valgrind.
Figure 4.8: Memory usage for FAS using Conjugate Gradient smoothing for
an 8192 ├Ś 8192 cell problem with ╬│ = 10
For sequential purposes, there is no doubt that Gauss-Seidel is the best smoother
for the non-linear elliptic boundary problem on the square domain of all those
tested. It uses less memory, and converges in less total runtime, and uses less
iterations. ItŌĆÖs both computationally and algorithmically superior to the alter-
natives.
However, the point of introducing these new smoothers is to explore how they
perform in a parallel setting. For that, see chapter 5.
It is not di’¼ācult to imagine that even larger problem sizes than those measured
simply crashed due to there not being enough memory, seeing how much memory
was being used.
Therefore, this report will limit itself to maximally testing
problems of size 8192 ├Ś 8192 on the unit square domain. In fairness, however,
this is a pretty massive problem to be solving your average dorm room PC.
==PAGE:85==
4.4 Tests
73
It is abundantly clear that FAS is capable of solving absolutely huge problems
compared to NewtonŌĆÖs Method if given the same amount of memory and pro-
cessing power. In this speci’¼üc case, the largest problem size could move from
4,198,401 unknowns for sparse NewtonŌĆÖs Method 67,125,249 unknowns for FAS.
ThatŌĆÖs an increase of of approximately 1600%, and yet total memory utilization
and compute time went down. Conclusively, it really pays o’¼Ćto construct a
solution method that is better designed to handle the problem given.

Chapter 5
Shared-Memory Parallel
Solvers
This chapter focuses on the parallellization of both NewtonŌĆÖs Method and FAS
for the use on the elliptic model problem on a shared memory system, such as
a modern Intel processor or IntelŌĆÖs Xeon Phi coprocessor.
The result of testing these implementations is presented in section 5.4.
OpenMP was chosen for the implementation of this method.
The specitica-
tions of OpenMP [Ope08] describes it as a standard useful for implementing
shared-memory parallel programs. As of this writing, it is still under active
development, but has had several major and feature rich revisions already.
In addition, OpenBLAS (containing all necessary LAPACK functions as well)
and SuperLU can be built using OpenMP. This is quite convenient, as testing
the parallelism as a function of the number of threads available simply becomes
a matter of setting the OMP_NUM_THREADS environment variable, and then all
libraries and software in the stack will respect that restriction.
Xiaoye S. Li [Li05] does warn that compiling SuperLU with a multithreaded
BLAS implementation can lead to instability or general slowness. In its place,
the developers have provided SuperLU_MT, which is SuperLU for multithreaded
applications. It will still expect a single-threaded BLAS implementation, but
==PAGE:88==
76
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
Xiaoye S. Li [Li05] claims that SuperLU_MT itself employs a large number of
tricks to use that sequential BLAS to factorize the sparse Jacobian in parallel.
Section 5.4.1.1 deals with this in more detail.
All the code implemented on the basis of the ideas discussed in this chapter, as
well as used in the tests, can be found in appendix D.1.2 and appendix D.1.3.
OpenMP was consistently set to scatter threads across as many cores as possible
using the KMP_AFFINITY=scatter environment variable.
5.1
AmdahlŌĆÖs Law, Parallel Speed-up, and Scal-
ing
This section serves as a short introduction to the theory that must be discussed
in order to understand the results of section 5.4.
The objective of parallel computing is to exploit the concurrency of the archi-
tecture to solve increasingly large problems in as short an amount of time as
possible. [Rot05] explains that, when dealing with a system wherein there are
multiple processing units that can execute code independently, one is said to be
dealing with a parallel system.
Modern CPU architectures often have many cores. Processors with such archi-
tectures are also called multi-core, or many-core, processors. Examples of this
are the Intel Core i7-4770K, which has 4 cores, or the Intel Xeon Phi, which has
60 cores. On such a single processor system with many cores, all the cores will
run at the same speed.
When all the cores have access to the same memory, such a system is also called
a shared memory parallel system by [Rot05].
Since this report deals with a
processor on a single die with access to the same system memory, this is the
case here.
In an ideal world, using P cores instead of 1 on a particular computational job
should result in the job being completed in the time 1/P. This would lead to a
P-fold increase in computational power.
Shared memory parallel system are often programmed using multi-threaded
programming. OpenMP provides a framework for doing exactly that, according
to [Ope08]. However, there are some caveats and challenges when developing
==PAGE:89==
5.1 AmdahlŌĆÖs Law, Parallel Speed-up, and Scaling
77
for such an architecture, as mentioned in section 1.1. They are repeated here
for completeness:
1. Su’¼ācient computation order independence - Since there are multiple threads,
it must be possible to carry out multiple computations at the same time
independently of one another.
2. Thread synchronization - Local data dependencies must be protected by
Thread synchronization. Threads must communicate, telling each other
when they have ’¼ünished computing the current batch of order-independent
operations before the next batch of operations can start execution.
3. Parallel Data Access - Every thread needs a local copy of the data it can
read from and write to. All the threads may read from any part of memory,
but the threads may not write to any memory another thread needs to
read from concurrently.
Because there are execution dependencies in any realistic implementation, it is
very rarely the case that parallelism will result execution being as many times
faster as there are hardware threads. Indeed, as will be described in section 5.2
and section 5.3, there are also execution dependencies in this program.
There is going to be some work that needs to be done entirely sequentially. This
includes a wide range of things, such as calling specialised functions, reduction
operations (everything must be reduced to 1 variable at the end, and only 1
thread can write to a variable at a time), and generally just cores having to wait
for each other for various reasons.
[Bro00] de’¼ünes the speed-up of a parallel program as
S(P) = T(1)
T(P)
(5.1)
where S(P) is the speed-up when using P cores, T(1) is the execution time on
one thread, and T(P) is the execution time on P hardware threads. In the
aforementioned perfect world, S(P) = P.
In virtually all realistic cases T(1) has both a portion that must be run sequen-
tially Ts, and one that can be run in parallel Tp. The runtime for the sequential
part of the code does not diminish when the parallel part is split up. The par-
allel portion can be sped up by 1/P, assuming no parallel overhead. Generally,
OpenMP should not introduce signi’¼ücant overhead according to itŌĆÖs speci’¼üca-
tions in [Ope08]. Large overhead is much more commonly seen in distributed
==PAGE:90==
78
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
computing, which this report does not deal with. The speed-up one can expect
is then:
S(P) = T(1)
T(P) Ōēż
Ts + Tp
Ts + Tp/N
(5.2)
This is known as AmdahlŌĆÖs Law according to [Bro00], which describes the best
possible speed-up that can be achieved by doing the work in parallel.
If the sequential part of the code is not much smaller than the part that can be
parallelized, there isnŌĆÖt much point, as not much speed-up would be observed
- no matter how many cores are available and no matter how well they are
utilized. In the case of FAS or NewtonŌĆÖs Method, however, the vast majority of
the code can be made parallel, which is what makes this idea compelling in the
’¼ürst place, as will be described in section 5.2 and section 5.3. As a result, we
am hoping to see good speed-up in section 5.4.
Another way to perceive the scaling e’¼āciency is through strong scaling. Accord-
ing to [SHA15], Strong scaling is a test in which the amount of computational
work stays the same but the amount of threads are increased. In this case, that
is achievable through simply letting the ’¼ünest grid size and non-linear coe’¼ā-
cient ╬│ stay constant, thus requiring the same amount of iterations on the same
amount of grids.
According to [SHA15], strong scaling is often used as justi’¼ücation for programs
that take a long time to run (i.e. is CPU-bound). This is the expected behaviour
for NewtonŌĆÖs Method according to [WWP09] (because it is O(n3) or (O(n1.5)
complexity), but not FAS (because it is O(n) complexity).
The program is
considered to scale linearly if the speed-up is equal to the number of processing
elements used - also known as the idealized situation mentioned earlier.
[SHA15] de’¼ünes the strong scaling e’¼āciency as:
SS(P) =
T(1)
P ┬Ę T(P)
(5.3)
The ’¼ünal way to perceive scaling e’¼āciency, which will be discussed in this report,
is weak scaling. [SHA15] describes a test of Weak scaling as a test in which both
the expected workload and the number of processing cores grow at the same
rate. For FAS, this can speci’¼ücally mean making the ’¼ünest grid twice as ’¼üne
in both cartesian directions. That is, set h = h/2 for the ’¼ünest grid. This will
quadruple the expected workload, as the complexity is O(n), and n = (N + 1)2.
To compute this, 4 times as many cores will be in use, and that will give an idea
of the weak scaling.
This type of measurement is justi’¼ücation for programs that take a lot of memory,
i.e. it is memory-bound according to [SHA15].
==PAGE:91==
5.2 A parallel NewtonŌĆÖs Method
79
Weak scaling e’¼āciency for going from 1 to 4 cores is as such:
WS(4) = T2h(1)
Th(4)
(5.4)
and from 1 to 16 cores:
WS(16) = T4h(1)
Th(16)
(5.5)
and so on.
5.2
A parallel NewtonŌĆÖs Method
As mentioned in section 4.2, BLAS, LAPACK, and SuperLU are all used through-
out the implementation of the sequential version of NewtonŌĆÖs Method. As men-
tioned in chapter 5, simply recompiling these libraries using OpenMP makes
these processes parallel.
NewtonŌĆÖs Method is, as described in section 2.1, a strictly sequential algorithm
otherwise. Recall from algorithm 1 that the residual must be retrieved before
convergence can be tested - and only if the residual is su’¼āciently large does it
make sense to compute the Newton step. And only once the whole Newton step
has been computed is it possible to take the step. Only once the step is fully
taken can the new residual be computed, and so on.
Most of the steps involved in NewtonŌĆÖs Method can theoretically be made fully
parallel up to n threads, where n is the number of unknowns, but NewtonŌĆÖs
Method itself is an iterative method, and the iterations have to be done in
sequence.
However these processes cannot be made entirely parallel: Finding the norm of
the residual (reduction operation), comparing it to the stop criteria, and various
system setup involved in the LU-factorization.
SuperLU_MT does not support Compressed Storage Rows, unlike the regular
SuperLU. However in this speci’¼üc application, this is not a problem, as the
matrix is symmetric, and Compressed Storage Columns are supported. How-
ever, for completeness, the implementation presented in this report will expect
Compressed Storage Rows, and then solve for the transposed system, which is
e’¼Ćectively the same as expecting the problem in Compressed Storage Columns.
==PAGE:92==
80
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
5.2.1
Function Evaluation
Setting up the right hand side of the function for the elliptic model problem
in parallel did not pose many additional challenges beyond what setting it up
sequentially did, as explained in section 4.2. Firstly, x and y had to be de’¼üned
for input into the source term function f(x, y) in accordance with the location
on the grid - which is to say linearly spaced with the distance h between each.
Since each value of x and y are independent of one another, this can be done in
parallel without any possible data races. In fact, both x and y can be set up
in parallel if enough threads are available. Since they have the length N + 1,
parallelism should be fully possible up to N + 1 threads.
After this is done, the source term itself can be set up. This is done through a
nested for-loop. Since each value of the source term, put into the array f, does
not depend on any value that changes as f is being set up, this can be done in
parallel easily up to n threads. There are no possible data races.
Parallellizing the computation of the residual is just as trivial. The entirety of
the current estimate to u is in memory at once as an array. The residual can
simply be constructed from the formula in eq. (2.3). Again, each output element
of r is entirely independent of other values in r and no elements in the estimate
of u change during the computation, nor do any of the constants, so there are
no possible data races.
Setting up the Jacobian is likewise trivial to parallelize, because during the
computation u does not change and the entire Jacobian is in memory at once.
As a safety measure, all the memory the Jacobian occupies can be set to 0
before values are put in. There are no possible data races. The implementation
presented only parallelizes for each row in the Jacobian, which means it is fully
parallel up to n threads.
When setting up the Jacobian in Compressed Storage Rows (CSR) for the sparse
SuperLU solver, which [Li05] claims expects this format (or Compressed Storage
Columns), problems arise because of a potential data race in the indexing.
It is easiest to explain this by including an example of the kind of code that can
go wrong in parallel when constructing a CSR matrix, as given in code listing
5.1.

rows must be known before the current row can be de’¼üned.

are in the matrix above the row currently being constructed beforehand, but this
requires a very large number of reduction operations, and thus the parallelism
wouldnŌĆÖt be much better for it.
This project does not focus on parallel de’¼ünition of sparse matrices, and Su-

this piece of code is entirely serial. It should be noted, however, that the Full
Approximation Scheme does not need the Jacobian to be explicitly constructed.
As a result, only NewtonŌĆÖs Method will su’¼Ćer from this problem, and only when
a sparse representation of the Jacobian is needed.
5.3
A parallel Full Approximation Scheme
FAS is set up very much like NewtonŌĆÖs Method, except rather than solving the
full problem as NewtonŌĆÖs Method does, it instead uses the multigrid idea. In
this implementation, the V-cycle was used. As can be guessed from ’¼üg. 3.9,
parallelizing the idea of the V-cycle itself is impossible.
This is because the steps of the FAS cycle almost always depend on the im-
mediately previous computation being completed.
It starts with smoothing.
Smoothers can internally have data racing issues, as explained in section 5.3.1
==PAGE:95==
5.3 A parallel Full Approximation Scheme
83
- but, even if they donŌĆÖt, each smoothing sweep depends on the result of the
previous smoothing sweep, so they must be done in sequence.
Then, the residual is computed for the ’¼üne grid, which depends on the estimate
of the solution to the ’¼üne grid vh, which was obtained by the last smooth-
ing sweep. Restricting the residual, which requires knowing the residual in its
entirety, follows immediately after.
A little parallelism is possible here because, while the residual is being computed
and restricted, vh must also be restricted to vH, which can be done immediately
when vh is available.
After that, however, the right-hand-side for the coarse grid is set up. Again, it
requires knowing the coarse grid residual and the coarse grid solution estimate,
and thus cannot be done before both of them are ready.
After this, because this is a V-cycling implementation, comes a recursive call
to the FAS Cycle itself. This gives a coarse-grid-corrected and hopefully better
estimate v of the true solution u. This process needs to complete in its entirety
before the correction can be computed, however.
This correction then needs to be prolongated, which requires knowing what
the correction is ’¼ürst. The result of that prolongation is then used to correct
the approximation.
After the approximation is obtained, post-smoothing is
performed.
So while FAS cycle itself cannot be parallellized in any meaningful way, each of
the workloads that FAS cycle has to are, usually up to n threads, where n is the
number of grid points on the grid currently being used by the FAS Cycle. This
of course includes the function evaluation, as discussed in section 5.2.1, although
some proesses there can only be parallelized up to N + 1 threads. This is very
similar to the situation with NewtonŌĆÖs Method. Other workloads involved in
the FAS cycle will now be discussed.
Naturally, simple vector-vector additions, such as computing the coarse grid
correction or correcting the ’¼üne grid approximation after prolongation is paral-
lellizable. According to [QXYY13], the implementations are parallel already by
virtue of the fact that it can be performed using a simple BLAS call, which is
implemented by OpenBLAS.
==PAGE:96==
84
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
5.3.1

To combat this issue, one may use what is known as red-black Gauss Seidel.

All in all, there are 2 major steps to it:

In particular, the solution of the triangular system can cause limits to the
amount of parallelism possible. The key idea of red-black Gauss Seidel is to
put the grid points into two groups, each identi’¼üed as either black or red nodes.
Any given node must be surrounded only by ones of the "opposite colour". The
idea is then to rearrange f and u such that all the "red nodes" are in the top,
and all the "black nodes" are in the bottom.
==PAGE:97==
5.3 A parallel Full Approximation Scheme

from [Kar00].

The Polynomial Chebyshev smoother, as described in section 3.2.4.1, is a lot
easier to make parallel than Gauss-Seidel. It does consist of a number of com-
putations which must be performed in sequence, however.
rnonlin, which must be evaluated ’¼ürst, can be evaluated in parallel on n threads,
as described in section 5.2.1. After this, the Eigenvalues of the non-linear compo-
nent of the Jacobian, which is a diagonal matrix as described in section 3.2.4.1,
can also be evaluated in parallel for the reasons described in section 5.2.1 for up
to n threads.
Finally, the largest Eigenvalue of J is found using a reduction operation. That,
however, is the last operation performed in this method that cannot be made
fully parallel. The rest is a sequence of matrix-vector products or vector-vector
additions that can be carried out entirely in parallel with up to n threads. This
fact, coupled with the lack of having to colour the nodes, is the main motivation
behind using Chebyshev polynomials as a smoother.
==PAGE:99==
5.3 A parallel Full Approximation Scheme
87
Finally, thereŌĆÖs the Conjugate Gradient Method smoother, as discussed in sec-
tion 3.2.5. This method is not as inherently parallel as any previous method
discussed. In addition to having a fairly long sequence of computations, much
like Chebyshev Smoothing (each of which are fully parallelizable), it funda-
mentally relies on several dot products, which are reduction operations. The
question is whether the algorithmic e’¼āciency of the method will be enough to
compensate for this shortcoming on a modern many-core architecture - only 2
sweeps were necessary for good smoothing.
With these things in place, the rest of the process is just vector-vector addition
and matrix-vector multiplication.
These are operations that the OpenBLAS
implementation [QXYY13] can perform in parallel.
5.3.2
Parallel Restriction
The standard 2D restriction, as it is presented in this report, is trivially paral-
lellized because it operates on a ’¼üne grid which does not change after entering
the function at all, and ’¼ülls the coarse grid data one element at a time in a loop,
each element being independent of other elements on the coarse grid. Simply
indicate to the compiler that the main loop can be performed in any order using
OpenMP, and it is done.
5.3.3
Parallel Prolongation
The standard 2D prolongation, as it is presented in this report, is trivially
parallellized because it operates on a coarse grid which does not change after
entering the function at all, and ’¼ülls the ’¼üne grid data one element at a time
in a loop, each element being independent of other elements on the ’¼üne grid.
Simply indicate to the compiler that the main loop can be performed in any
order using OpenMP, and it is done.
5.3.4
Coarse Grid Solving
Since all the tests will be using a smoother to solve the coarse grid problem,
how easy it can be made parallel depends on which smoothing algorithm is used.
The same restrictions and caveats apply as when using parallel smoothers for
the other grids, as described in section 5.3.1.
==PAGE:100==
88
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
Each smoothing step will have to be taken in sequence when performing coarse
grid solving.
5.4
Tests
Only computational e’¼āciency will be considered for these tests. The algorithmic
e’¼āciency has been established in section 4.4.1 and has not changed in the parallel
execution at all. The same methods are being used across the board from a
mathematical standpoint.
This section is split into two main chapters:
1. Testing on the home system using the same compiler and optimization
’¼éags described in section 4.4, but now also using OpenMP and letting the
number of threads exceed one.
2. Testing on the Xeon Phi using the Intel C Compiler as well as OpenMP.
The methods tested are:
1. NewtonŌĆÖs Method with LU-factorization carried out on a sparse CSR Ja-
cobian Matrix
2. The Full Approximation Scheme using 5 sweeps with the Newton-GaussSeidel
smoother. A new global linearisation is carried with each sweep.
3. The Full Approximation Scheme using 5 sweeps with the Chebyshev-
polynomial smoother. Only 1 linearisation is carried out per smoothing.
4. The Full Approximation Scheme using 2 sweeps of the Conjugate Gradient
based smoother. Only 1 linearisation is carried out per smoothing.
NewtonŌĆÖs Method with a full Jacobian is not included because it is uninteresting,
as it can be compared directly to the SuperLU version. It uses a very long time
to compute and takes up so much memory that large grid sizes cannot be tested.
Keep in mind, throughout this test, that the entire FAS program, including
setup of the Jacobian and residual, and even computing the size of the error
and residual, is tested for parallelism, not just the FAS method itself. While
the smoother is what is expected to take the longest time, in accordance with
==PAGE:101==
5.4 Tests
89
the results in section 4.4.2, and is being compared between runs, even a per-
fectly parallel smoother would not result in perfect scaling, because some other
processes are not perfectly parallel, as explained in section 5.3.
5.4.1
Testing on Haswell Desktop CPU
The system used for these tests are described in appendix A.1.
Testing on the home system included the very simple process of testing the code
for 1, 2, 4, and 8 threads using a 4096 ├Ś 4096 problem with ╬│ = 10 (’¼üg. 5.2).
This test is designed to show the speed-up and strong scaling for my system
when this code is run on it.
Gauss-Seidel is the fastest method across the board. While the Conjugate Gra-
dient method can compete with it for 1 or 2 threads, it rapidly falls behind with
its poor strong scaling. This can most likely be attributed to the inner products
that are very common in the method (as well as many other Krylov substace
methods).
Chebyshev is quite a bit slower than Gauss-Seidel, but it scales about as well
in the strong scaling, and gives a roughly similar speed-up. Somehow, it even
manages to have a strong scaling above 100% on 2 threads.
This is almost
certainly a ’¼éuke, as this should be completely impossible due to AmdahlŌĆÖs Law,
even by the most optimistic expectations. So all the tests were run 5 times, but
this result was very consistent.
Other than that, they scale roughly equally well, with Gauss-Seidel coming out
slightly ahead.
Something quite noteworthy is the poor scaling of going from 4 threads to 8
threads.
This is because the processor used for these tests does not have 4
physical cores, but instead features Intel HyperThreading, as shown on IntelŌĆÖs
Ark Website, linked in appendix A.1. According to [ZBC+04], Hyperthreading
can result in small to signi’¼ücant performance gains, but it ultimately never
confers anywhere near as much bene’¼üt as extra physical cores, so this result is
largely expected.
A second test was carried out for 1, 2, 4, and 8 threads using a 8192 ├Ś 8192
problem with ╬│ = 10 (’¼üg. 5.3). This is mainly to ensure that the same or a sim-
ilar scaling pattern is observed for an even larger problem. As can be seen, this
is mostly the case. The strong scaling is a little lower for all 3 implementations,
and Gauss-Seidel remains the clear winner.

Having these two di’¼Ćerent results, one for a problem with approximately 4 times

on this system, as shown in table 5.1.
This test con’¼ürms a very interesting fact: The weak scaling of the implemen-
tation with Chebyshev Polynomial smoothing is better than that with Gauss-
Seidel. In general, for all the tests, the version of FAS with Chebyshev Polyno-
mial smoothing is generally slowest, but also to scales best, until hyperthreading
comes into play.
This is most likely a result of the fact that the Chebyshev polynimial smoother
can be very easily vectorized, compared to the more complex checkerboard-like
structure of red-black Gauss-Seidel. The compiler is made aware of this through
memory alignment and compiler ’¼éags, and most likely creates a version of the
program that takes advantage of this, making it faster and easier to schedule
the tasks for the threads, and thereby improving parallel performance ever so
slightly.
The Chebyshev polynomial smoothing method does have a very interesting

trade-o’¼Ćcompared to the Gauss-Seidel smoothing method.
Whereas one of
them requires a good estimate of the absolute value of the largest Eigenvalue to
be e’¼Ćective and the other doesnŌĆÖt, the other one requires "colouring" the grid.
This can become beyond just slightly annoying when using the method in a
multigrid context, for obvious reasons.
Grouping the grid into red and black nodes is relatively easy for a simple prob-
lem, such as this one. However, for grids that are non-uniform, it can become
extremely di’¼ācult according to [ZC06] - particularly as many di’¼Ćerent grids
that a non-uniform in di’¼Ćerent ways.
On the other hand, deriving the largest Eigenvalue is not necessarily easy, either.
In the case of this simple non-linear problem, it was relatively cheap to work out
using WeylŌĆÖs Inequality, but it might not always be. [GKV11] mentions various
methods exist to solve this problem of working out the largest Eigenvalue quite
e’¼āciently. These include methods based on a modi’¼üed power method, or using
the Arnoldi iteration. However, doing so might be expensive, so when evaluating
these two methods against each other, it is useful to consider how easy it is to
colour the nodes for use in Red-Black Gauss-Seidel ’¼ürst.
Both Gauss-Seidel and Chebyshev smoothing scale very well for large-scale
shared memory processors. However, depending on the nature of the problem,
a constant amount of compute power may be saved by picking Gauss-Seidel
over Chebyshev smoothing, but equally many work-hours may be lost making
Gauss-Seidel work if it is chosen for a discretization onto which it is di’¼ācult to
apply.
Generally, the Conjugate Gradient Method is only really useful when not con-
sidering parallelism and not wanting to colour the nodes, either. But in that
speci’¼üc case, it is very good.
Conclusively, one should strongly consider what tool one takes into use to deal
with the speci’¼üc problem before making a conclusion about which is best.
5.4.1.1
Sparse NewtonŌĆÖs Method test
A test of the sparse implementation of NewtonŌĆÖs Method was carried out. Two
implementations were created and tested. The comparison of these might be
interesting, as FAS is memory-bound, but NewtonŌĆÖs Method is compute-bound,
and we are dealing with modern massively parallel architectures, where, as noted
by [GKV11], ’¼éoating point operations are cheap, and memory access may be
quite expensive.
==PAGE:105==
5.4 Tests
93
One implementation uses the "regular" SuperLU for sequential systems, but
with a multithreaded BLAS implementation underneath, in the form of Open-
BLAS. [Li05] generally advises against this, but makes it clear that some speed
may be derived from this, so it is worth exploring.
The other uses a multithreaded version of SuperLU, called SuperLU_MT, which
must use a single-threaded version of BLAS underneath - or it crashes or be-
comes unreliable according to [Li05] and personal experience.
Figure 5.4 shows the strong scaling observed for a fairly small ’¼ünest grid dis-
cretization of the elliptic non-linear boundary value problem. Immediately obvi-
ous is that using the single-threaded SuperLU but with multi-threaded BLAS,
results in basically no scaling whatsoever.
This does motivate the need for
SuperLU_MT, and [Li05] did warn that this was not an intended use of the
software.
Despite the relatively small size of the problem, the solution time is about the
same as much larger problems using FAS. In fact, FAS is able to solve a system
with up to 16 times as many unknowns in the same amount of time, using the
same amount of processing power, even when using SuperLU_MT.
The code used to perform these tests can be found in Appendix D.1.2 and D.1.3.
Because the entire Jacobian must be stored in memory at once, as well as storage
for an upper and lower triangular matrix for the factorization, SuperLU uses
much more memory than FAS for the same sized problem. SuperLU_MT makes
this worse than SuperLU, to the point where a 1024 ├Ś 1024 problem was the
largest one that could be solved on the machine in appendix A.1 without running
entirely out of memory.
While NewtonŌĆÖs Method has many outstanding qualities, including in particular
the ability to solve systems of equations that do not have a smooth solution (see
2) and in many ways cannot be compared directly to FAS in a general sense,
the result shown on ’¼üg. 5.4 is a big part of what motivated this piece of work.
Conclusively, for these types of problems, FAS is both faster and uses less mem-
ory, even in an environment where it is generally more limiting to be memory-
bound than compute-bound. FAS also scales well to the point where NewtonŌĆÖs
Method is not expected to be able to compete with it in run-time. NewtonŌĆÖs
Method will no longer be considered for the rest of this report.

5.4.2
Testing on Xeon Phi
[JJ13] describes the Xeon Phi as a parallel co-processor from Intel, which is
designed to make it easier to get a lot of performance out of highly parallel
applications, particularly in the area of scienti’¼üc computing.
Using IntelŌĆÖs Many Integrated Core Architecture, abbreviated Intel MIC, it runs
standard x86 programs in a Linux environment. Because of this, as [JJ13] is
keen to point out, it is possible to write a parallel program using standard
C methods, including OpenMP for instance, much easier than it would be to
rewrite the program for graphics cards using languages such as OpenCL or
CUDA.
[JJ13] recommends using the Intel C Compiler for compiling programs for the
Xeon Phi. When compiling, [JJ13] recommends use the Intel Math Kernel Li-
brary for BLAS and LAPACK implemtations, as they are optimized speci’¼ücally
for the Xeon Phi, and as a result should be very fast.
Basically, the only hurdle in getting the parallel program described in the previ-
ous section, and given in Appendix D, running fast on a Xeon Phi is to switch to
==PAGE:107==
5.4 Tests
95
the Intel compiler and use the -mmic -openmp -O3 -mkl -parallel compiler
’¼éags, which are mentioned by [Sof13].
The compiler should then automati-
cally take advantage of the massively parallel architecture and the Single Input
Multiple Data (SIMD) and vectorization subsystems found in the Xeon Phi.
Due to the system set up at DTUŌĆÖs compute cluster, a few additional steps were
needed. These, along with a description of the Xeon Phi used for these tests,
are described in appendix A.5.
While the Xeon Phi contains 60 physical cores, [JJ13] claim that it can perform
more computational work with up to 4 threads per core, and that it is often a
good idea to essentially pretend there is one more physical core - so 61 "cores" in
total. According to [JJ13], this comes as a result of the high-bandwidth many-
core architecture of the Xeon Phi, which also has very fast communication speeds
between the cores themselves. If one or more threads complete the workload
assigned to them faster than the others, another thread can help pick up the
slack and make them work again.
According to [Cor15], a theoretical max bandwidth of 320GB/s can be achieved.
It also has a 30MB L2 cache with even higher memory bandwidth, which should
drastically reduce the overhead of inter-processor communication and hopefully
allow the page faults, that are inevitable with some of these smoothing mthods,
to not be so punishing.
That combination of being able to run up to 244 simultaneous threads and
having such a huge memory bandwidth should really allow FAS to shine. While
it is unrealistic to expect as good scaling as was observed for the 4-core system,
due to solving for the coarsest grid (with only 9 unknowns!) and other such
matters, it should still be fast.
So, testing was carried for 1, 4, and 8, 16, 32, 61, 122, and 244 threads using a
1024├Ś1024 problem with ╬│ = 10, a 2048├Ś2048 problem with ╬│ = 10, 4096├Ś4096
problem with ╬│ = 10, and a 8192 ├Ś 8192 problem with ╬│ = 10.
These results were used to attain strong scaling and speed-up data, and these
results can be seen in ’¼üg. 5.5, ’¼üg. 5.6, ’¼üg. 5.7, and ’¼üg. 5.8.
One immediately noticeable weirdness is the result on ’¼üg. 5.8, where Gauss-
Seidel is able to achieve higher than 100% strong scaling. Again, this result was
consistently found in an average of 5 test-runs, and I am unable to explain it.
Beyond that, the Xeon Phi pulls o’¼Ćsomething more that is also quite odd.
Here, Chebyshev is the fastest method on few threads, but also scales the worst
- exactly opposite to the result observed on the 4-core Haswell architecture.
==PAGE:108==
96
Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
This was not expected. What was expected is that Gauss-Seidel would remain
overall fastest, and it is - at all ’¼ünest grid sizes, but also that Chebyshev would
scale roughly the same.
But it gets even weirder: Up to about 32 cores, the scaling looks somewhat
decent for all the di’¼Ćerent implemenations of FAS, but then it drops down before
all hardware threads (physical cores) are in use! On top of that, in almost all
cases, the Xeon Phi actually becomes slower when HyperThreading is taken into
use as well. Gauss-Seidel seems to be able to bene’¼üt from 2 threads per physical
core, but it cannot bene’¼üt from 4 threads per physical core.
That is extremely disappointing. One might expect that, with poor scaling,
using all physical cores would not make much of a di’¼Ćerence - but for it to
become downright slower implies that there is a lot of inter-processor overhead.
That does seem like a reasonable explanation. As described in section 5.3, FAS
does consist of a fairly long sequence of problems that must be carried out in
order. Each of these problems can be parallelized, but the V-cycle and multigrid
idea itself cannot. Consequently, the threads constantly have to communicate
that they are done with their current task, and are ready to cross the barrier.
Conclusively, the more cores are in use, the more threads will wait around for
each other to ’¼ünish the current task, and the more signals will have to be sent
between cores to communicate when a task is done, which takes processing
power.
These sorts of scenarios are exactly why [Bro00] described AmdahlŌĆÖs Law as
being a bit too optimistic, because it does not take this extra workload into
consideration.
Another way to view the result is in terms of weak scaling. The weak scaling
looks really good for small amounts of threads, but does not for large amounts
of threads and larger problems, exactly as before. This scaling is going to be
somewhat imprecise because it works with 1 thread, 4, threads, 16 threads, and
61 threads instead of 64 threads. However, it is precise enough to get an idea
of the general trend, as seen on ’¼üg. 5.9.
As discussed in section 3.7, this problem is heavily memory-bound, and thus
relies a great deal on the memory bandwidth to run fast. A natural question
to ask here is that, since inter-processor communication is supposed to be very
fast, could there be any other factor at play that has an e’¼Ćect on this?
According to [Sol13], whcih cites a test known as STREAM, by John D. Mc-
Calpin, Ph.D., there is. In fact, as the total number of threads running on the

Shared-Memory Parallel Solvers
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
The report has presented an e’¼ācient and fast implementation of FAS, as well
as tests to run numerical experiments, for many-core processors.
The report shows using a Chebyshev polynomial smoother for a non-linear prob-
lem in a FAS context. The runtime is approximately twice that of using Gauss-
Seidel for the same problem, but the method removes the need to perform
red-black colouring of the grid points.
Scaling and performance on the home system showed that, when using a system
of 4 physical cores, almost a 4 times speed-up with both Chebyshev Polynomial
Smoothing and Gauss-Seidel smoothing can be achieved.
The Conjugate Gradient methodŌĆÖs strong scaling was about 320% on 4 cores.
Even though Krylov Subspace methods are generally fast, and are often used as
smoothers for FAS, their heavy use of inner products limit their scalability in
many-core situations.
Additionally, the code can be run on IntelŌĆÖs Many Integrated Cores architec-
ture, such as the Xeon Phi. The results of using the Xeon Phi to run the code
showed good scaling up to about 32 physical cores using the Conjugate Gra-
dient smoothing method in FAS, and up to all physical cores using the other
smoothers.
==PAGE:114==
102
Conclusion
When using multiple threads on each core, the total running time becomes
higher on the Xeon Phi, indicating that attempting to use the Hyperthreading
features does not result in better performance in this case. This appears to be
a result of the Xeon Phi getting worse memory bandwidth when Hyperhreading
is taken into use.
For both the Haswell CPU and the Xeon Phi the memory was aligned, the
vector engines were used extensively, all the hardware threads were in use, and
so was Hyperthreading technology. If the inter-processor communication can be
reduced and memory bandwidth exploited better, this method can be sped up
on the Xeon Phi.
The numerical experiments show that, in the bast case, the code presented in
this report is able to solve the model non-linear elliptic boundary problem with
a 8192 ├Ś 8192 grid on a standard consumer PC in about a minute.
When comparing to NewtonŌĆÖs Method, clearly FAS has lower running time due
to its computational complexity for solving discretizations of non-linear elliptic
boundary problems where the Jacobian is always positive de’¼ünite given the use
of any of the presented smoothers. However, NewtonŌĆÖs Method can solve many
other types of problems, and is as such more generally applicable.
6.1
Future Work Suggestions
1. This report does not deal with domain-decomposition and MPI at all.
Ghysels et al [GKV11] already did this for linear multigrid, including with
Chebyshev Polynomial smoothing, and the results showed large speed-up.
These methods could also bene’¼üt FAS.
2. Domain-decomposition might also be usable to o’¼Ć-load to multiple Xeon
Phis at once. With the combined power of a modern Xeon CPU, and many
Xeon PhiŌĆÖs employed speci’¼ücally to solve the massively parallelizable tasks
that arise in a FAS implementation, great speed-up may be acheived.
3. There are many more types of parallelizable smoothing techniques out
there which might be interesting to test, including Truncated Neumann
Polynomial Smoothing.
4. The hardware used for testing in this report was slightly out of date. New
versions of the Xeon Phi have come out since then.
5. More work is needed to optimize this method for the Intel Many Integrated
Core architecture.

A.1
The Hardware
The development done on this project was primarily done in a Linux environ-
ment on the undersignedŌĆÖs home computer. Here are the basic speci’¼ücations for
that machine:
ŌĆó Motherboard: Gigabyte GA-Z87X-UD3H
ŌĆó Central Processing Unit: Intel Core-i7 4770K running at 3.9GHz.
ŌĆó Memory: 2x8GB Kingston Savage memory running at 2400MHz. CL11,
dual-channel, non-ECC.
ŌĆó Graphics Processing Units: 2x GeForce GTX 680 with 2GB of memory
each on a 8x PCI-e lane each.

ŌĆó Linux kernel 3.19.0-22-generic x86_64 GNU/Linux
==PAGE:116==
104
Setting up the environment
A.2
Installing the Operating System
The ’¼ürst step is to install Ubuntu 15.04. For this, an 8GB USB 2.0 pendrive
and UNetBootIn was used. It was installed in UEFI mode.
Once Ubuntu is installed and boots up the ’¼ürst time, the ’¼ürst thing that will
happen is that the software update pops up. Let it do its thing, and restart.
In the case of the aforementioned computer, one should also install the NVIDIA
graphics drivers. While Ubuntu does o’¼Ćer built-in graphics drivers in the form
of Nouveau, they are rather slow and buggy despite the considerable and com-
mendable e’¼Ćort of the developers. This not only has an e’¼Ćect on GPU perfor-
mance, but also on CPU performance to run the driver. For this reason, I chose
to install the NVIDIA proprietary driver.
To install it, do the following steps:
1. Find the newest version of the driver at the NVIDIA homepage which, as
of this writing, is version 346.59.
2. To install it, Xorg must be shut down. To do this, go to a terminal by
pressing ctrl+alt+F1.
3. You will be asked to login again. Do so.
4. Navitage to the downloads folder using cd, e.g.: cd Downloads
5. Shut down Xorg. In Ubuntu, Xorg is run by the lightdm system service.
This can only be done as administrator. sudo stop lightdm
6. You will be asked to type in your password again. Do so.
7. Install the driver as an administrator. sudo ./NVIDIA*
8. Go through the install process. If it says the package script failed, ignore
that and continue. When it asks to install the 32-bit OpenGL libraries,
select Yes.
9. Restart the computer using sudo reboot
Once the computer starts up again, log in. The software was installed in the
home folder, which one can do again. Place it in the home folder.
==PAGE:117==
A.3 Model Problem Version Software
105
A.3
Model Problem Version Software
Now, install the basic packages needed for the software.
The ’¼ürst thing is required to build anything. It contains a C/C++ compiler
and the standard C libraries. It is called build-essential.
The second is BLAS and LAPACK, or Basic Linear Algebra Subroutines and
Linear Algebra Package. It was decided to use OpenBLAS built from source
and optimized for the Haswell architecture.
In order to compile anything, Ubuntu will need the build essentials. To install
them, open a terminal. This can be done using the shortcut ctrl+alt+T. In this
terminal, type sudo apt-get install build-essential. It will most likely
suggest to install additional packages. This is okay, proceed. The version of
GCC which is downloaded is 4.9.2, which has full support for these optimizations
using the march=haswell ’¼éag.
The project folder itself in this case has libopenblas.so included in the folder.
This is OpenBLAS built with OpenMP. To test using Sequential Version Soft-
ware, set the variable OMP_NUM_THREADS to 1.
The third is SuperLU. This software uses SuperLU for testing NewtonŌĆÖs Method.
This is because SuperLU can use sparse matrices for solving the system. Not
using sparse matrices is a serious liability which will cause the system to run
out memory even for relatively small problems when using NewtonŌĆÖs Method.
It was built from source, downloaded from SuperLUŌĆÖs website. The latest ver-
sion of version 5.0 for single-threaded applications, and 3.0 for multithreaded
applications according to its UserŌĆÖs Guide [Li05].
Once installed, compiling the sequential code already becomes possible.
To
build it, simply change directory to Sequential folder within the project, and
type make. To run it, type ./rls.exe. To make a debug version, type make
debug.
Debugging of the program can be done using gdb (GNU Debugger), which was
installed by build-essential. First type make debug, then type gdb dbg.exe and
debug away.
Pro’¼üling of the program can be done using GProf (GNU gprof).
Pro’¼üling
symbols are added by GCC when building using make profile, then type
./prof.exe.
==PAGE:118==
106
Setting up the environment
If something goes wrong in the build process or the program refuses to build for
some reason, type build clean to clean it up and start over.
A.4
Extra debugging and pro’¼üling tools
In the development of this project, a few extra tools were used to optimize it.
First and foremost, Valgrind was used directly from the terminal. To install
it, type sudo apt-get install valgrind.
Valgrind helps identify memory
leakage and was therefore instrumental in the optimization process.
Furthermore, Eclipse was used. Eclipse has 2 nice features for the purposes of
this project. The ’¼ürst is that it is easily possible to import the make’¼üle project
to the point where it is possible to run the executable through the built in
debugger, which I found a lot easier than using the debugger from the terminal.
The second is that it has GProf integration.
To install eclipse, simply run sudo apt-get install eclipse eclipse-cdt.
To install the GProf integration, install it from within Eclipse from the top
menu Help -> Install New Software.
Choose all sources, then sleect GProf
integration. At this point, after running the program with pro’¼üling symbols
using ./prof.exe from the terminal, a ’¼üle called gmon.out will be created.
This ’¼üle can now be opened in Eclipse, and can show which functions used a lot
of time once given which ’¼üle created it.

Appendix B
Neumann Polynomial
Smoothing
Another type of Polynomial smoother, which was not explored in any depth in
this report, was the Neumann Polynimial smoother.
However, since signi’¼ücant time was spent with it, here is a little information on
what was going on.
The Neumann Polynomial Smoother is, as the name suggests, a preconditioner
which can be used as a smoother in a Multigrid context. Apparently, it is very
good on GPUŌĆÖs for linear problems according to Vuik et al [VGvG+13], so it
was natural to assume it would be good for a non-linear problem on a Xeon Phi.
Vuik et al [VGvG+13] describe the Truncated Neumann Series Polynomial
Method as a preconditioner for systems of linear equations. It turns out that this
method, in addition to being a very e’¼ācient way of solving linear (or Newton-
linearized, as in this case) equations, also causes smoothing when applied to a
set of linear equations, including such things as discretizations of elliptic model
problems with added random noise.
The method may be applied to the linear system of equations
Je = r
(B.1)
==PAGE:122==
110
Neumann Polynomial Smoothing
The Truncated Neumann Series Polynomial Preconditioner is described as the
following by Vuik et al [VGvG+13]:
MŌłÆ1 = KT DŌłÆ1K
(B.2)
where D is the diagonal of J and K = I + PQ
k=1(ŌłÆ1)k(LDŌłÆ1)k. Here, L is
the strictly lower diagonal of J, and Q is the degree of the truncated Neumann
Polynomial.
The method is stable if ŌłźLDŌłÆ1ŌłźŌł×< 1 according to Vuik et al [VGvG+13].
The correction found by computing e = MŌłÆ1r can then be applied to the current
estimate, such that it can be updated:
u ŌåÉu + MŌłÆ1r
(B.3)
This can be written in algorithmic form, as shown on algorithm 12.
Algorithm 12 Truncated Neumann Series Polynomial Smoothing
Get Initial estimate u and Jacobian at that location J.
Get initial residual: r ŌåÉf ŌłÆJu
Compute inverse of diagonal elements in Jacobian, DŌłÆ1.
Get lower triangular of Jacobian: L
Let K = I + PQ
k=1(ŌłÆ1)k(LDŌłÆ1)k
Let MŌłÆ1 = KT DŌłÆ1K
return u ŌåÉu + MŌłÆ1r
The result of this smoothing can be seen on ’¼üg. B.1. It is immediately apparent
that this smoother works incredibly well; the error term is smooth even with a
1, perhaps 2, degree Polynomial.
The primary advantage of this method is that there is no need to compute the
maximum or minimum Eigenvalue or colour the grid cells or anything like that.
Furthermore, the method relies on Matrix-Matrix and Matrix-Vector products,
which should enable strong scaling.
However, its biggest strength is also its biggest problem. Because it acts on
Matrix-Matrix products, and because the exact behaviour for sparse matrices
is di’¼ācult to write down (though not di’¼ācult to compute), this method will
almost certainly require that the Jacobian is de’¼üned, at least in sparse format,
in memory. This makes FAS considerably less lean, and also further slows the
method down signi’¼ücantly because of the increased memory access needed to
retrieve numbers like ŌłÆhŌłÆ2 over and over again.
The method did receive an implementation in MATLAB, found in Appendix
D.2.4, but has not received any testing for speed in C and OpenMP.

Appendix C
Theory of Error Smoothing
Trottenberg suggests [TOS00] starting by performing a Fourier expansion of the
error. Consider the linear version of the elliptic model problem 2.3, for example.
It can have its error on a certain grid with grid spacing h written as a function
of the discrete variables x and y written as, according to Trottenberg [TOS00]:
eh(x, y) =
nŌłÆ1
X
k,Ōäō=1
╬▒k,Ōäōsin(kŽĆx) sin(ŌäōŽĆy)
(C.1)
for (x, y) ŌłłGh. The functions
ŽĢh,k,Ōäō= sin(kŽĆx) sin(ŌäōŽĆy)
(k, Ōäō= 1, ..., n ŌłÆ1)
(C.2)
are the discrete Eigenfunctions of the discrete 5-point star stencil operator shown
on ’¼üg. 1.5.
The fact that the error becomes smooth after some iterations, as observed in
section 3.2, means that the high frequency components ╬▒k,Ōäōsin(kŽĆx) sin(ŌäōŽĆy)
with k or Ōäōlarge become small after a few iterations, whereas the low frequency
components ╬▒k,Ōäōsin(kŽĆx) sin(ŌäōŽĆy) with k or Ōäōsmall barely change at all.
==PAGE:126==
114
Theory of Error Smoothing
Consider now the problem on both a ’¼üne grid with grid spacing h and one one
a coarse grid with grid spacing H = 2╬║h, where ╬║ ŌłłN. For ╬║ = 1, which will
be used throughout this report, this is called standard coarsening, [TOS00]. To
de’¼üne what, in this case, becomes high frequency noise and what becomes low
frequency noise, consider the Eigenfunctions
ŽĢk,Ōäō,
ŽĢnŌłÆk,nŌłÆŌäō,
ŽĢnŌłÆk,Ōäō,
ŽĢk,nŌłÆŌäō
(C.3)
where n is the number of grid cells. Note that they coincide on a grid on the
coarse grid like this:
ŽĢk,Ōäō(x, y) = ŌłÆŽĢnŌłÆk,Ōäō(x, y) = ŌłÆŽĢk,nŌłÆŌäō(x, y) = ŽĢnŌłÆk,nŌłÆŌäō(x, y)
(C.4)
In other words, these 4 Eigenfunctions cannot be distinguished on the coarse
grid with grid distance 2h. Essentially, for k or Ōäō= n/2╬║ the Eigenfunction
vanishes on the coarse grid. The de’¼ünition of low and high frequency noise are,
as such:
In the context of the elliptic model problem, let k, ŌäōŌłł{1, ..., n ŌłÆ1}. Denote ŽĢk,Ōäō
to be an Eigenfunction (or a component) of low frequency if max(k, Ōäō) < n/2╬║
and of high frequency if n/2╬║ Ōēżmax(k, Ōäō) < n.
According to Trottenberg [TOS00], coarse grid correction will work well if only
low frequency errors are removed by the smoother. High frequency errors, how-
ever, do not need to be removed. The smoother does not, however, need to solve
the problem to any real accuracy.

46

47

51

59

//Computes the kronecker product of A and B and puts it in C.

//A must be allocated with size Awidth*Aheight

void chraux_kron(double *A, size_t Awidth, size_t Aheight, double *B,
size_t Bwidth, size_t Bheight, double *C)
154
{
155

#pragma omp parallel for collapse(4)
158
for (iA = 0; iA < Aheight; iA++)
159
{
160
for (jA = 0; jA < Awidth; jA++)
161
{
162
for (iB = 0; iB < Bheight; iB++)
163
{
164
for (jB = 0; jB < Bwidth; jB++)
165
{
166
C[(iA*Bheight + iB)*Awidth*Bwidth + jA*Bwidth + jB] =
A[iA*Awidth + jA]*B[iB*Bwidth + jB];

}
168
}
169
}
170
}

return;

int solver_newton
(void (*fun)(double *x, double *f, double *r, double *

//Number of evaluations

65

67

74

75

76

}
89
90

91

{

abs_tol = opts[2];

//Get right hand
99

100
101
//Get residual values
102

103
104

105

106
norms_2[iter] = first_norm;
107

==PAGE:137==
D.1 Open MP Parallel Code. Language: C, OpenMP 3.1
125
109

//Take step
111

//Underlying true function
29
double Tfun2(double x, double y, void* p) {return (xŌłÆx*x)*(yŌłÆy*y);}
30
double Qfun2(double x, double y, void* p) //Evaluation of left hand side of
PDE.
31
{
32

33

34

//Right hand side of the elliptic model problem

void elliptic_model_problem_rhs(double *f, size_t *dimsizes, size_t ndims,

{

//Left hand side of the elliptic model problem.

void elliptic_model_problem_lhs(double *v, double h, double *fv, size_t *
dimsizes, size_t ndims, void *p)

{

)+(j+1)] ŌłÆv[(iŌłÆ1)*(N)+j] ŌłÆv[(i+1)*(N)+j]) + gamma*v[i*(N)+j
]*exp(v[i*(N)+j]);

}

}

//Chebyshev Polynomial of degree k. Input lambda.
318
double ChebP(double lambda, int k)
319
{
320
return cosh((double)k * acosh(lambda));

//Prolongates the input grid, vH, moving the data to vh.

//A prolongation is an operater which transfers data from a coarser grid to a

518
//This version only works for 2ŌłÆdimensional problems.
519
//This prolongation operator uses linear interpolation between the nearest
neighbours.

void elliptic_model_problem_Prolongate(double *vH, size_t *dimsizes_H, //Input
521
double *vh, size_t *dimsizes_h, //vh is

522

//Compute size of coarse in this case, restricting from h to 2h
578

}

function v2 = prolongate(v)

function u = ChebSmooth(u,f,h,gamma, steps)
100
101
[r_nonlin] = myfun(u,h,gamma,f);
102
103
n = size(u,1);
104
105

121
T = @(lam, n) cosh(n*acosh(lam));
122
p = 1/d*r;

124
for n = 1:steps,
125
126
e = e + p;

h^(ŌłÆ2)*(e(iŌłÆ1,j) + e(i+1,j) + e(i,jŌłÆ1) + e(i,j+1)));

function u = ChebSmoothLin(u,f,h,gamma, steps)

151
%Using Weyl's inequality. This is not quite working but estimate seems good

function u = ConjGradSmooth(u,f,h,gamma,N)
99
r = myfun(u,h,gamma,f);
100
p = r;
101
Jp = zeros(size(u));
102
e = zeros(size(u));
103
104
prodr = r(:)'*r(:);
105
for k = 1:N,
106
for i = 2:(size(u,1)ŌłÆ1)
107
for j = 2:(size(u,2)ŌłÆ1)
108
Jp(i,j) = ((h^(ŌłÆ2)*4 + gamma*( exp(u(i,j))+u(i,j)*exp(u(i,j)) ))*p
(i,j) ŌłÆ...
109
h^(ŌłÆ2)*(p(iŌłÆ1,j) + p(i+1,j) + p(i,jŌłÆ1) + p(i,j+1)));
110
end
111
end
112
%
Jp(:) = J*p(:);
113
alpha = prodr/(p(:)'*Jp(:));
114
e(:) = e(:) + alpha*p(:);
115
r(:) = r(:) ŌłÆalpha*Jp(:);
116
prodrnew = r(:)'*r(:);
117
beta = prodrnew/prodr;
118
prodr = prodrnew;
119
p(:) = r(:) + beta*p(:);
120
end
121
122
u = u + e;
123
end

function u = NeumannSmooth(u,f,h,gamma,N)
99
[r, J] = myfun(u,h,gamma,f);
100
101
J = ŌłÆJ;
102
103
dim = length(u(:));
104
105
diagJ = diag(J,0);
106
% D = spdiags(diagJ, 0, dim, dim);
107
Dinv = spdiags(diagJ.^(ŌłÆ1), 0, dim, dim);
108
109
L = tril(J, ŌłÆ1);
110
111
% norm(L*Dinv, inf)
112
113
K = speye(dim);
114
for k = 1:N
115
K = K + (ŌłÆ1)^k*(L*Dinv)^(k);
116
end
117
118
Minv = K'*Dinv*K;
119
120
u(:) = u(:) + Minv*r(:);
121
122
123
end
